Preface
We want to thank our supervisor, Anders Damgaard, for his incredible guidance
throughout the thesis process. His feedback and comprehensive knowledge in
the engineering ﬁeld, as well as constructive critique, provided us with a clearer
idea of the thesis direction, allowing us to work and present a subject that we
are very passionate about. We hold Anders in the highest regard and can highly
endorse him as a supervisor for interdisciplinary engineering projects.
Moreover, we would also like to thank Thomas Fænø Mondrup, Technical
Consultant from Green Building Council Denmark, for initiating the thesis
idea and inviting us to the oﬃce and knowledge-sharing their ongoing "CO2
Framework". We would also like to express our gratitude to Lisbet Michaelsen,
Sustainability Coordinator at Technical University of Denmark and furthermore
John Kepny-Rasmussen, Energy Consultant, and Hejdi Gregersen, Project
Leader, from Københavns Almene Boliger, who took the time to answer our
questions and provided data for the building case studies.
Finally, our gratefulness goes to our family and friends who showed excellent
support,
during COVID-19,
when we could not advance and for their
understanding when we were working long nights on the project.
To us,
synergising the subjects of energy systems, environmental assessments and
architectural planning was a very exciting journey and a fulﬁlling experience
that culminated in 5 and ½ years of education.
We sincerely hope that our
thesis will evoke interest and add a diﬀerent perspective to an essential topic
concerning climate change and sustainability planning in the built environment.
Enjoy the reading,
Christian, Dan & Troels

==PAGE:8==
Abstract 
The building industry is a massive emitter of global greenhouse gases that are released into the 
atmosphere today. Cutting emissions through energy consumption is therefore necessary across the 
building industry if the planet wants to have a chance of overturning climate change. A way to curb 
emissions for buildings and streamline its way towards aligning with the Paris Agreement targets is 
to redefine the relationship between buildings, energy and emissions. This study aims to decompose 
and decipher how existing buildings in Denmark can work towards complying with global 1.5 °C 
goal and reach net-zero emissions. This is done by investigating the energy systems on a national 
scale, the energy system on a building level and the energy and emissions related to the production 
of building materials, with all elements comprehended in a holistic and highly synergetic manner. 
 
Based on the scenario analysis tool TIMES,  Denmark’s energy system development between 2018-
2050 was created, constrained by a CO2 budget and a political target. The overall heat-saving 
strategies, CO2-factors and CO2 target on a building level were derived, and the CO2 target was 
tested on two existing buildings for future renovation. On a building level, LCA was applied to the 
materials renovation strategies using SimaPro, while considering the dynamic influences of the 
renewable transition from the energy system. Furthermore, the operational energy from the two 
existing buildings was calculated on a design level stage by applying ‘Rockwool Energy Design’. 
The roadmap to decarbonising buildings was supported by applying and developing a framework 
which encapsulated and recalculated the whole building process, from the national energy system 
to the buildings embodied materials and operational energy, and into CO2 emissions.  
 
For both building cases, 1650 and 1683 different renovation strategies were tested respectively 
between the period 2018-2050 to assess when and how these strategies would comply with the CO2 
target. The results indicated that there is a huge demand for buildings to act promptly and implement 
an ambitious level of renovation, otherwise risk not adhering with the targets. Buildings must act in 
time during the energy systems development, as the results indicated that the renovation strategies 
was too late to implement after the year 2028 for both cases. The results also indicated that all 
buildings must be assessed as a unique case as the embodied carbon and operational energy behaved 
differently for each case, as well as they developed differently through time.  
 
Further research is, however, needed to expand the scope of the framework to incorporate more 
renovation strategies and additional building types, to gain a more holistic understanding of 
decarbonising the building sector.

Deﬁnitions
Carbon
Carbon dioxide (CO2)
Carbon Budget
The remaining allowed carbon that can be emitted and
still comply with a maximum of 1.5 °C temperature
increase with 66% probability
Carbon Negative
When absorbing more carbon dioxide than emitting
Carbon Neutral
A state when the CO2 emission is equal to the uptake,
also called net-zero
Carbon Oﬀsetting
A practice of putting funds towards organisations
which help the environment by lowering or reducing
carbon emissions
CO2 Pathway
Describes how carbon dioxide emissions develop over a
speciﬁc time horizon
CO2 Roadmap
Describes how carbon dioxide emissions develop over a
speciﬁc time horizon
Embodied Carbon
Carbon dioxide (CO2) related to the life cycle of a
material
Net-Zero Emissions
A state when the CO2 emission is equal to the uptake,
also called carbon neutral
Neutralisation Factor
A factor that represents the carbon dioxide absorption
==PAGE:16==
CONTENTS
Oﬀ-Site Energy
Energy produced on central energy production plants
such as CHP plants and land-based PV systems. After
which the energy is supplied to the end-user through
the national district heating or power grid
On-Site Energy
Energy produced locally and directly supplied to the
end-user, such as individual gas boilers or solar
PV-panels on buildings
Operational Carbon
Carbon dioxide (CO2) related to the energy used in
the operating phase of the building

1.1
Background
The global temperature has increased signiﬁcantly in the last century. It has
caused changes to the climate around the world, such as sea levels rising
and natural disasters happening more frequently.
Numerous studies have
demonstrated that the primary driver for the increase in the global temperature
is the utilisation of fossil fuels in energy combustion, coupled with the world
population’s reliance on energy, which has evolved into almost every aspect
of human life and its activities.
The problem is furthermore ignited by
industrialisation and a rising world economy (Ritchie et al., 2017).
These concerns have given rise to international commitments and collaborations
to reduce global warming, such as the Paris Agreement. The agreement has called
for each country participating to lowering the risk of environmental impacts
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
from climate change, by aiming for a global temperature increase to well below
2°C above pre-industrial levels and targeting the limit to raise the temperature
of not more than 1.5°C by year 2100 (Rogelj et al., 2018).
These planned
commitments to lowering the impacts are deﬁned in national submissions of
National Determined Contributions (NDCs) related to the Paris Agreement.
Recent research has demonstrated that if participating countries are going
to be successful in alleviating with the rise in temperature, they must focus
on encompassing all sectors as a part of their solution when lowering their
greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. The studies indicate that the world only has
a ﬁnite amount of GHG emissions left, deﬁned as a CO2-emissions budget, to
reach the target of 1.5°C or 2°C (Rogelj et al., 2018).
According to McSweeney et al. (2017), if the world continues its energy
consumption with no improvements, there is a chance that the CO 2 budget
can be exceeded in 4.1 years (1.5°C) and 19.1 years (2°C) from 2017.
This
reality poses a signiﬁcant requirement for decarbonisation, where all sectors in
a country, such as building, transportation, industry, agriculture and ﬁsheries,
must act promptly and improve its end-use eﬃciency. At the same time, countries
must also transition towards utilising renewable resources and replace fossil fuels
in end-use technologies as well as in the supply technologies.
Some sectors are responsible for releasing greater amounts of CO2 emissions
into the atmosphere than others. One of these is the building industry, which
is accountable for producing CO2 emissions throughout its entire lifecycle.
Research indicates that the building and construction industry is responsible for
39% of all energy-related emissions, with 28% of the emissions associated with
operations (heating, cooling, electricity etc.) and 11% associated with embodied
materials (extraction, manufacturing, end-of-life) (Abergel et al., 2018).
The
building sector thus has huge potential for decreasing its CO2 emissions which
can play a key role in the temperature rise and the GHGs that are emitted today.
This is primarily due to the reliance on fossil energy supply from the national
energy system in building operation.
Existing literature in the ﬁeld of energy systems focuses primarily on modelling
and forecasting how to optimally invest in robust renewable supply technologies
going forward in a national energy system context (Mathiesen et al., 2015; Barisa
et al., 2018). From those models, forecasting is made on how to replace fossil fuels
and intelligently transition towards an energy system of renewable technologies
2
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
so that political targets can be reached.
However, most of these studies do
not go into depth on a sectoral level, such as how speciﬁc buildings should
align within a larger-scale context to holistically work towards curbing emissions.
The current eﬀorts to limit emissions for buildings rank high on the industry’s
agenda. Strategies to decarbonise existing buildings mainly focus on reducing
the operational CO2 emissions by increasing energy eﬃciency through building
refurbishment or by adding on-site renewable energy sources (Akande et al.,
2016; Couty et al., 2017). Eﬀorts to curb the embodied CO2 emissions focus
mainly on incorporating circular approaches such as reusing waste in a building
(Tingley et al., 2018; Ng et al., 2015).
At the moment, the energy system, operational CO2 and embodied CO2 are
looked into within an individual perspective when improving and creating
buildings. This approach, however, neglects the holistic context of transitioning
towards renewable energy supply, as well as the common political target of 1.5°C
that must be met to limit climate change.
As a consequence, buildings are
currently operating and being built without an informed aim in terms of CO2
emitted, which can spark positive change towards decarbonisation in buildings.
Without a clear emissions roadmap and framework, the building industry will
risk lacking the necessary means to implement initiatives required for reducing
the CO2 emissions and achieve the global targets.
This is a study of cross-disciplinary investigation in the ﬁeld of architectural,
environmental and energy systems engineering. It investigates an overall strategy
for Denmark and how the energy system and the building sector can develop in
the future constrained by political targets. Furthermore, it explores concrete
strategies and solutions so building owners can improve existing buildings,
in terms of operational and embodied CO2 through application of a holistic
framework, to advance towards a future of net-zero emissions.
1.2
Aim
The study investigates three topics which are; energy systems development
on a national scale, CO2 emissions related to operational energy consumption
and embodied CO2 emissions for building materials. It aims to decipher how
the building industry in Denmark should comply with the 1.5°C target and
consequently its emissions roadmap to reach net-zero emissions in 2050.
By
3
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
synthesising the three topics, it aims to explore an evaluation framework for
existing buildings to make informed decisions for renovation while considering all
three areas simultaneously. Investigations on renovation strategies will support
the framework, incorporating operational and embodied CO2 emissions, with the
aim of decarbonising buildings in Denmark.
The project aims to answer the following research questions:
1. What is the optimal combination of heat saving scenarios for existing
buildings and the energy systems development for Denmark in order to
comply with the target of 1.5°C budget and reach net-zero emissions?
2. What are the CO2 roadmaps for diﬀerent existing building types, and the
consequential limit value for the speciﬁc building?
3. How
should
existing
building
typologies
streamline
strategies
for
operational and embodied CO2 emissions to stay within the 1.5°C budget?
4. How can an evaluation framework, combining energy system development,
operational CO2 and embodied CO2 emissions for buildings, be valuable as
an assessment tool going forward?
1.3
Scope
The research was limited to Denmark and the Danish building industry with
the CO2 budget counting from the period 2018-2050 and was narrowed to only
assessing CO2 emissions. Due to wanting a full dataset to minimise the number
of inconsistencies, it was decided to start this study from 2018, where most of
the data was obtainable. The decision to limit this study to only one nation
was due to the complex nature of modelling multiple energy systems, but also
due to the signiﬁcant diﬀerence in construction practices and methodologies in
other countries. The study was moreover limited to Danish building practises
and regulations (The Danish Building Code 2018) and national policies and
agreements (Energiaftale 2018).
The framework was scoped to only existing
buildings, with the framework testing renovation strategies on two types of
buildings. The whole study applied a framework based on design level detail
for both building an energy system.
4
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.4
Approach
The study is an exploratory research which aims to acquire insights into the
building industry’s current state of emissions and to explore and identify the
critical aspects of the problem with suggestions on what can be improved, and
how it can be improved. The study consists of three central topics, which in the
initial stages of the research is looked into separately:
1. National Energy System
2. Operational Carbon in Buildings
3. Embodied Carbon in Buildings
The literature review addresses these topics and further investigates the current
status quo and the gap in knowledge. This study applies a mixed approach of
quantitative and qualitative methods to answer the research questions. It uses
modelling and evaluation tools and creates its own holistic framework combining
the three topics to answer the questions.
The analysis of the national energy system applies a quantitative approach using
TIMES modelling tool.
It requires a large and detailed data sample size to
determine the national energy systems development. It is based on technical and
economic parameters, demand projections and CO2-budget constraints. Using
this data, the energy model determines the supply technology development and
the total emissions allowed for the building sector to adhere with CO2 budget.
This is called the net-zero emissions roadmap.
The model is supported by a mixed-method analysis of two selected building
typologies:
a multistorey (residential) building and an oﬃce (commercial)
building.
From the selected buildings, the current energy consumption is
emphasised, to outline energy-saving strategies within building operation.
For embodied carbon, a quantitative assessment of building materials and
components for energy-saving strategies are made and calculated using LCI
databases in SimaPro.
Lastly, the three topics are synthesised, and the buildings’ holistic CO2
consumption is applied into a framework.
The framework will be based on
CO2-factors from the future energy system and the CO2-emissions budget for
Denmark, and will consider how each building can reach net-zero emissions within
the constrained carbon budget.

and applies it on two building case studies. Additionally, it assesses the time-
related impact on building materials and components and analyses the embodied
carbon for renovation strategies. Framework Results synthesises the ﬁndings from
the modelling and analyses them in the frameworks to explore strategies and
solutions for each building case. Lastly, the Discussion chapter proposes how
strategies, solutions and frameworks can lead buildings towards decarbonisation
that aligns with political targets.

2.1
CO2 Emissions
The concentration of GHG emissions consists of; carbon dioxide (CO2), methane
(CH4), nitrous oxide (N2O) and other gasses. These emissions are released into
the atmosphere through activities on life requiring energy. The concentration of
GHG emissions in the earth’s atmosphere has increased throughout the years,
leading to the greenhouse eﬀect and prompting environmental problems such as
global warming and climate change (Gray, 2015). CO2 is the most critical gas in
the GHG composition. Studies have shown that we only have a ﬁnite amount of
CO2 emissions that can be released into the atmosphere before the temperature
increases to a level that causes negative consequences to the biosphere (Rogelj et
al., 2018). The upper limit is called a "CO2 Budget" and is determined through a
historical accumulation of CO2 emissions and its impact on global temperature.
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
The global CO2 budget can be separated into national budgets so countries can
set speciﬁc climate mitigation goals. There exists a range of methods to estimate
the budget, which each gives diﬀerent limits of CO2 emissions (Hausfather, 2018).
One of them is the global CO2 budget set by IPCC 2015, which is the budget that
is internationally acknowledged when targeting net-zero transition. IPCC deﬁnes
net-zero CO2 as when CO2 emissions are balanced globally by CO2 removals over
a speciﬁc period. CO2 removals cover technologies such as CCS/U and natural
carbon uptake by oceans and land biosphere (Rogelj et al., 2018).
On a building level CO2 is also gaining more focus across the industry. World-
GBC, for example deﬁnes, a net-zero building as:
"A net-zero embodied carbon building (new or renovated) or infrastructure asset
that is highly resource-eﬃcient with upfront carbon minimised to the greatest
extent possible and all remaining embodied carbon reduced or, as a last resort,
oﬀset in order to achieve net-zero across the lifecycle." (Adams et al., 2019)
This deﬁnition, however, only consider the embodied carbon aspect on building
level as it includes carbon oﬀsetting as a solution for buildings to obtain net-zero.
It does not take into account the holistic context of operational energy as well
as the energy system.
According to Energy & Climate Intelligence Unit (2020), as many as 103 countries
are discussing a net-zero target in their national legislation, while 20 countries
have already pledged to target net-zero CO2 by 2050, with Denmark one of those
countries.

==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Past studies demonstrate that there is a clear relationship between CO2
emissions, energy consumption and economic growth (Akdag et al., 2020; Omri,
2013).
CO2 emissions can be curbed by cleaner production and combustion
in energy sectors, by transitioning into renewable technologies and substituting
fossil technologies. Another critical part is increasing overall energy eﬃciency
across sectors, as less energy consumption will result in less CO2 emissions
(Kamal et al., 2019).
2.2
Energy Systems
An energy system is a system where energy, such as electricity, heat or fuel,
is supplied to its ﬁnal users which can be "end-users" or "end-sectors". These
systems can be local and smaller systems, such as a building that is oﬀset by
renewable energy as researched by Wang et al. (2018) or large-scale systems
like a national energy system (Farzaneh, 2019). A national energy system is
diﬀerent from a local building energy system, which, e.g. only has occupants
as its end-users for energy supply. In contrast, a national energy system has
more associated processes and multiple end-sectors. These end-sectors are, for
example, residential, commercial, industry and transportation. Production of
energy is driven by the demand in these sectors but operates through processes
such as conversion and distribution, including losses, before ﬁnal consumption,
which can be seen in the ﬁgure below.
On-site energy system
• Local energy transformation 
• Energy directly to end-users
DKK
Energy to end-sectors 
from off-site production
Off-site energy conversion, 
transformation & distribution
Oﬀ-site energy system
Figure 2.2: Two types of energy systems. Inspired by IEA-ETSAP (2020)
Past studies indicate that production and consumption of energy are integral
in the role of climate change, and by implementing national energy system
9
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
modelling, which has grown excessively in studies over the years (Yan et al.,
2019; Berntsen et al., 2017), governments can consider possible future scenarios
and actions needed to regulate and transition towards its objectives (Farzaneh,
2019). The future transition towards renewable technologies is therefore crucial,
also for the building industry, as it is highly dependant on the energy supply
from the national energy system. It is therefore vital for buildings to include the
holistic context of energy supply and its development in the pursuit of carbon
neutrality.
2.2.1
Energy Modelling
The main objective of modelling a national energy system is to create a
representation of reality and to foresight the system’s future development.
Modelling can contribute to informed decisions for how the system that is
analysed should operate or how policies can be developed to support the analysed
problem.
The goal of an energy model is to solve a research problem through a set of
mathematical algorithms and computation.
Energy modelling of a national
energy system contributes to clarifying possible scenarios given certain conditions
and constraints (Farzaneh, 2019). The results can then be applied to understand
which actions can be made to solve the research problem. It ultimately leads to
insight(s) into a possible future based on the given conditions and constraints
applied in the energy model.
Past studies have demonstrated that energy
modelling can answer problems such as modelling diversity of electricity supply
to understand which optimal technologies that should be prioritised (Berntsen
et al., 2017), or, e.g. feasibility studies on GHG to create a mitigation roadmap
for a nation etc.
To reach its future objective an energy system needs a plan. There are several
strategic approaches for planning the future and determining the actions needed
to achieve the objective. Strategic approaches such as forecasting, backcasting
and scenario planning are widely recognised approaches in organisational
planning (Allison et al., 2011), and are also employed in the energy systems
modelling sphere. The approaches are visualised below:
In energy systems modelling, the planning approaches are transformed into a
computational framework and is deﬁned as "general purposes" by Hourcade et
10
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Future
Present
Future
Present
Future
Present
Forecasting
Scenarios
Backcasting
Predict most 
likely future
Explore alternative 
futures
Assess feasibility 
of desired future
Figure 2.3: Planning approaches. Adapted from Allison et al. (2011)
al. (1996). The choice of general purpose, or modelling purpose, depends on the
objective of the problem and what the problem wants to address. Energy models
based on a general purpose can be divided into three models:
Forecasting Models uses past data such as data on population, economy, emissions
etc. which are correlated to the objective and extrapolates the data to predict
future outcomes. These models are often used, e.g. to predict and forecast CO2
emissions based on past data on variables which are closely correlated, such as
economic growth, urban population, energy, consumption etc.
Scenario Analysis Models explores multiple future pathways for an energy system
based on a reference scenario (starting year of the model). The energy scenario
is driven by projections on production, consumption, prices, investments,
technology mixes etc. These models are often used to create multiple future
scenarios on a range of, e.g. CO2 targets (or budgets) in order to determine a
range of possibilities moving forward.
Backcasting Models uses a backward approach to develop desired future outcomes
for an energy system. The backward approach diﬀerentiates from the forecasting
approach, by starting from the objective and moving backwards to the present to
help identify what measures and actions that need to be done. These models are
often used to target a future objective, such as a carbon-neutral society before a
certain date.
Energy models also have a speciﬁc purpose (Farzaneh, 2019), which delves into
the mathematical framework of how the model is driven and what it reﬂects. If
11
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
the model focuses on the ﬁnal energy consumption in the system and considers
the demand-side’s factors which can aﬀect the result, the model is a Demand-Side
Model. If the model focuses on the energy supply technologies and on determining
the optimal design of supply technologies, then the model is a Supply-Side Model.
If the model focuses on both supply and demand and the full set of processes in
an energy system, the model is an Integrated Model.
Besides a model’s purpose, energy modelling is dominated by two analytical
approaches to fulﬁl the energy demand from an economic perspective. There is a
bottom-up engineering approach and a top-down economic approach (Jacobsen,
1998).
Aggregated 
variable
Aggregated 
variable
Top-down 
approach
Bottom-up 
approach
split of aggregated variable 
dependent on prices
aggregation
components
components
components determined by 
projected demand and technical 
and economical data
Figure 2.4: Analytical approach. Adapted from Jacobsen (1998)
The Top-down modelling is an economical approach that describes the whole
economy of the system, which is deﬁned by Jacobsen (1998) as an aggregated
variable.
It emphasises on achieving equilibrium in supply and demand by
applying macroeconomic modelling principles and techniques on data such as
capital, price and income (Jacobsen, 1998). The modelling splits the aggregated
variable and ﬁnds opportunities to substitute diﬀerent components (Helgesen,
2013).
The Bottom-up modelling is an engineering approach which is widely used in
energy analysis and planning. It incorporates an intensive amount of technical
and economic detail on diﬀerent components in the system, including present
and future components (Jacobsen, 1998). In contrast to the top-down approach,
which uses the whole system as the starting point, the bottom-up approach
12
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
starts with the technologies meaning the disaggregated variables. The bottom-
up approach seeks to ﬁnd the most optimal supply technology combination(s)
for the whole system and ends with an aggregated variable.
There is a considerable diﬀerence between the two approaches, which identiﬁes
the system in diﬀerent ways and thus yields diﬀerent results (Jacobsen, 1998).
This has led to some past studies combining both approaches, as they ﬁnd them
complimentary (Barker et al., 1995; Helgesen, 2013; Dai et al., 2016) However,
there are still other studies that prefer to use the bottom-up approach (Kavgic
et al., 2010; Zhou et al., 2013).
In bottom-up and top-down modelling there are diﬀerent types of approaches
(Helgesen, 2013). The most common are optimisation models, which seeks the
most optimal solutions for technologies based on the total cost of the system.
Another common model is simulation models, which integrates information and
addresses how a system works under given circumstances. It does not reﬂect the
most optimal solutions, like the optimisation model. There are diﬀerent types
of energy system models available. An overview of each models speciﬁc purpose,
analytical and methodological approaches can be seen in Appendix A.
2.2.2
CO2-factors allocations
In an energy system,
it is necessary to allocate the fuel consumption
between power and heat in cogeneration plants when producing environmental
declarations for electricity and district heat to decide a CO2-factor. In principle,
that is an unmanageable task, but there exist diﬀerent methods to allocate the
CO2-emissions with individual strengths and weaknesses. Energinet.dk (2019)
presents three types of methods. One is the Energy Content Method relates all
emissions directly to the energy output. In this way, relatively more emissions
link to heat production compared to the following methods. All losses in the
production are also shared respectively between power and heat.
Another
method is the Thermal Eﬃciency Method, which assume that heat is produced
with eﬃciencies of 125% or 200%, respectively. This method relates more CO2-
emissions to power production. All losses are also associated with electricity.
The last method is the Energy Quality Method, which calculates how much extra
electricity that could have been produced if no heat was produced. It sees heat
as a by-product and is thus saying that electricity is produced of higher quality
(Energinet.dk, 2019) (Appendix B)
13

A building emits CO2 in all stages throughout its life cycle. A typical life cycle
of a building, consists of three main stages interpreted by Goldstein et al. (2018)
as: pre-use, use and after-use. These stages, consists of sub-stages which can be
categorised into operational and embodied CO2 emissions. The exact processes
that are connected to these CO2 categories depends on the methodology and
boundary chosen for the speciﬁc study.
When reducing CO2 emissions in buildings, most research studies focus on short-
term reduction. These studies do not employ the time parameter, such as how
the energy supply technologies will develop in the future and how it will aﬀect
operational and embodied CO2 emissions. The studies focus indirectly on CO2
reductions, such as immediate optimisation of the energy demand or focuses on
reusing materials in buildings resulting in lower embodied CO2 emissions (Beltran
et al., 2018).
Reducing CO2 emissions in buildings requires coordinated actions (WorldGBC
et al., 2019) due to the processes and the time parameter aﬀecting each other.
14
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
For example when implementing a new building envelope or installing building
integrated solar panels the operational CO2 will be reduced, but it requires an
investment of embodied carbon ’upfront’ to install the new measures.
2.4

Figure 2.5: Embodied carbon in building parts and components from processes.
Inspired by Goldstein et al. (2018)
Buildings have CO2 "embodied" in its building parts and components.
The
amount of embodied CO2 in each building component diﬀer and depends on
its composition of materials and the CO2-related processes connected to the
materials. CO2 emissions are released throughout the material’s supply chain and
is associated with manufacturing, production and end-of-use processes (Monahan
et al., 2011).
The building parts consists of material layers, which composes the physical
structure that must meet functional, structural and energy demands of the
speciﬁc building. These demands deﬁne the choice of materials, which is decided
by its structural and thermal properties. The building envelope is vital as it is
the external shell of the building and consists of several building parts such as
the roof, external wall and external ﬂoor. It is directly coupled to the heat loss
(energy loss) and has thermal minimum requirements for new and old buildings
which must be met, set by Danish building regulations (The Danish Construction
et al., 2020).
The volume and types of materials used for the building envelopes have an
inﬂuence on the total embodied CO2 emissions.
Simultaneously the building
envelope also has an inﬂuence on the energy consumption. The ’right’ variations
for the envelope is therefore important as there is a clear correlation between
deciding the embodied CO2 and the operational CO2 emissions (Ibn-Mohammed
et al., 2013).
15
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Past studies have shown that focus towards upfront embodied carbon reductions
are increasing in the early stages of building construction (Darby et al., 2012).
There have been studies suggesting that it is possible to ﬁnd reductions in the
early stages of building development by choosing the best design alternatives for
building with the lowest embodied CO2 as studied by Marsh et al. (2018). There
has also been increasing focus on mitigating embodied CO2 in construction by
applying circular economic principles, and thus introducing a reusing economy
to the sector which in general have shown to have a lower embodied CO2 than
new conventional materials such as Tingley et al. (2018) and Wiik et al. (2018)
investigates.
2.4.1
Life Cycle Assesment in Buildings
The embodied CO2 of buildings are mostly assessed using the life cycle assessment
(LCA) methodology. It is the basis for quantifying the environmental impact of
processes, materials and components and for identifying areas of improvement
(Beltran et al., 2018). There are several standardised methods for measuring
embodied CO2 in buildings. Standards such as ISO 14040 (2009), ISO 14044
(2006), EN 15804 (2012), EN 15978 (2012) and ILCD handbooks are methods
that are generally used in the industry.
The methodologies diﬀerentiate in
system boundaries, and often only one method is chosen for a study to achieve
consistency.
Past studies have demonstrated limitations of LCAs in terms of ﬂexibility with
time frames and lifespans. For buildings, a typical time horizon of 100 years for
the building industry is applied for the LCAs with all impacts related to one
pulse emission. A study by (Levasseur et al., 2010) addresses an approach which
takes into account the temporal issue and ultimately improves the accuracy of
the general LCA by providing a method which takes into account the timing of
emissions for the speciﬁc studies.
Measuring the embodied CO2 in building materials and components is diﬃcult
as most of the data is continued variables.
A study by Huijbregts et al.
(2003), concludes that most LCA studies on embodied CO2 in buildings, rely
on single-point estimates which represents an average numerical output. But
there can be uncertainty connected to this for reused materials which cannot
rely on general terms. Another challenge in estimating the embodied CO2 in
construction materials, is the lack of necessary information and uncertainty of
16
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
how technological advancement will develop and aﬀect the future processes of
the materials. These uncertainties vary in data and are important to incorporate
when understanding and estimating the life cycle embodied CO2 (Gantner et al.,
2018).
However, for the building sector, it is uncommon for LCAs to consider the
uncertainties. A review of 77 studies by Beltran et al. (2018) showed that most
studies excluded uncertainties. The studies that treat uncertainty in LCA often
applies a two-point, three-point or probabilistic methods such as Monte Carlo
simulations to address the parameter uncertainties.
2.4.2
Assessing Lifespan
The lifespan of materials, building parts and building types are essential when
assessing embodied CO2 emissions. However, international standards of values
in this topic does not yet exist. Research by Aagaard et al. (2013), addresses
this problem by calculating the lifespan of a range of materials in building parts
and building types. The study is based on national data and the international
framework ISO 15686 (2011) for assessing the average lifespan of building
materials.
There are diﬀerent types of lifespans which are all important to distinguish.
There is a technical lifespan; that is the technical period where the building part
no longer can deliver its function. This lifespan is dependent on the material
quality, climate impact, usage, maintenance etc.
Furthermore, there is the
functional lifespan; which is the period from when the building part is needed
until a sudden change. This type of lifespan is dependent on societal changes,
technology development etc.
There is also the economical lifespan, which is
the period until it is feasible to substitute the building part. Lastly, there is
an aesthetic lifespan, which is the period until the building part no longer can
satisfy due to appearance (Aagaard et al., 2013).
2.4.3
Linear vs. Circular Economy
There are two types of economy models in a production chain, which are very
diﬀerent. The most common type is the linear production. It starts with resource
extraction and ends in the discard phase, which means that most products ends
17
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
as waste too early in terms of material lifespan and quality. This type of economy
generates waste in a very fast manner and is not sustainable in the long run. The
circular model maintains the product for usage in multiple generations. This
model utilises the material quality and its lifespan, by recirculating the product
into a new generation until the material value drops and can no longer be of
functional use (Stahel, 2019). The models can be seen below:

Figure 2.6: Simpliﬁcation of production processes of two economies known in
the building industry. The linear economy (left) and circular economy (right).
Inspired by MacArthur et al. (2013)
Circular principles can be applied to buildings to reduce embodied CO2.
Preliminary study by Andersen et al. (2019) demonstrates the environmental
impact of circular economy approaches in the building industry and how it
can be implemented with LCA to reduce the embodied CO2. The study is a
simpliﬁcation and underlines the lack of empirical data available for circular
economic approaches in the building sector. This is general for most research
studies of circular economy in the building industry as the term is still at it
infancy (Hossain et al., 2018). Despite its critical considerations, studies have
demonstrated clear potentials of reducing environmental impact in a building by
reusing building waste materials and recirculating into a another building life
(Eberhardt et al., 2019; Ng et al., 2015). The clear demonstrations of beneﬁts
has led to more studies advancing towards how this can be incorporated more in
18
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
the future.
2.5
Operational CO2
Buildings are either supplied by on- or oﬀ-site energy. The on-site energy includes
individual boilers for space heating (SH) and domestic hot water (DHW) for
cooking and showering. These are currently fueled with a high share of fossil
fuels.
Renewable on-site options are generally related to solar energy, which
can provide both heat and power directly to the end-user (see ﬁgure 2.2). The
oﬀ-site energy, i.e. district heating and electricity supply, is provided by the
national energy system which supplies a large volume of building stock. The
operational carbon for buildings supplied with oﬀ-site energy thus depends on
the developments on a larger scale context through the energy system.
Exhaust Air
Supply Air
Transmission 
Losses
Solar Gains
Figure 2.7: Energy demand in a building. Inspired by MacArthur et al. (2013)
The energy demand in buildings, is deﬁned as the energy required to provide
occupants with a comfortable environment and the energy required to meet
the building’s function.
To provide occupants with a comfortable indoor
environment, building’s have mechanical systems, that provides ventilation,
heating and water, while it has electrical applications providing lighting to the
buildings users (C. M. Hvenegaard et al., 2007; C. Hvenegaard et al. (2008)).
19
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
The buildings must meet a certain quality. A range of studies has been made
regarding indoor environmental quality (IEQ), which shapes the building around
thermal quality, lighting quality, air quality and acoustic quality. Mechanical
installations abate these four qualities, and the benchmark values must be met
by Danish standards and guidelines of concerning the indoor environment, such
as DS/EN 15251 (2007) and the more updated version DS/CEN 16798 (2017).
These standards are, however, more directed towards working environments in
commercial buildings.
2.5.1
Calculation of Energy Consumption
The energy consumption in the Danish building industry is calculated by
applying holistic energy simulation programs. The programs incorporate detail
for calculating the energy consumption in buildings, such as the building envelope
and building installations. Some of the software that is standardised and applied
by consulting engineers, architects and contractors during several building phases
for veriﬁcation, is tools such as BE18 (Aggerholm et al., 2005) or Rockwool
Energy Design.
There has also been a rise of 3D modelling and simulations which has made
the calculation of energy consumption much more integrated. The simulations
are dynamic modelling tools which give a fast calculation response on a
range of parameters.
Simulation programs are central in today’s integrated
environmental analysis and for designing and retroﬁtting buildings with low
energy consumption. Buildings which applies these dynamic modelling methods
can navigate between the most energy optimal designs (Carmo et al., 2016).
Past studies have demonstrated gaps between the calculated and actual energy
consumption in buildings. Some of the reason for these gaps is that people have
individual user behaviour and comfort levels, which are reﬂected in the buildings
energy consumption. For example, the studies show that some prefer a warmer
indoor climate causing a higher heating consumption than projected. There are
furthermore also gaps in how the buildings are built contrary to how it is planned
which create unforeseen circumstances. (Kragh et al., 2017)
20

￿￿
3.6
Operational Carbon . . .
￿￿
The thesis was initiated in collaboration with Green Building Council Denmark
(DK-GBC), as independent research prompted by the pilot phase ’CO2
Framework’ for buildings. The framework by DK-GBC encapsulated the CO2
emissions related to the operational stage in buildings, and aimed to project
and reduce CO2 emissions for existing buildings based on a speciﬁc building’s
operational consumption. This study was inspired by the framework by DK-
GBC and thus investigated how the scope could be expanded by incorporating
additional elements, to give a clear and comprehensive evaluation of how
buildings in Denmark would have to evolve to comply with the 1.5°C target.
To achieve this, the future energy system was determined through energy
modelling, followed by an investigation on operational energy and embodied
CO2 for two existing buildings.
The following chapters address the research
approach, research methods of data collection and analysis applied for each topic.
It furthermore describes our self-developed framework that would allow buildings
to comply with the 1.5°C target and achieve net-zero carbon emission.

Figure 3.1: Convergent mixed-method design. Inspired by Creswell et al. (2003)
The objective of the research was to develop, evaluate and apply a framework
for future scenarios of buildings in Denmark constrained by the ﬁnite amount of
’allowable’ emissions set by the 1.5°C pathway. To clarify the research questions,
the thesis incorporated elements of both qualitative and quantitative approaches
to investigate how the energy system would develop over time. It furthermore
examined how these dynamics would aﬀect future building life cycles, such
as building operation (operational carbon), and production and demolition of
materials and components (embodied carbon).
Because the thesis integrated
both elements of research methodologies concurrently, the study was a mixed-
method research (Creswell et al., 2003).
The research of the energy system
was quantitative research that used an inductive bottom-up method starting
with data and enabling the scenarios to emerge itself, constrained by the 1.5°C
pathway. The investigation on embodied carbon was a quantitative analysis of
renovation strategies’ development through time for reused and virgin building
materials and components. The research on operational carbon was a mixed-
method research that used energy data from building case studies and used
energy design tools to propose and register current consumption of buildings. The
framework merged all results and was applied to each building case study, with
the eﬀect of each strategy encapsulated through the aggregation of operational
and embodied carbon.
22
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.2
Research Approach
Knowledge of how engineers work and think is essential for understanding how
the research for this study was constructed and carried out. The primary aim of
an engineer, as opposed to other research disciplines such as e.g. social science
and humanities, is to ﬁnd optimal solutions to problems by identifying and
validating (Robinson, 1998). The research was conducted with a similar mindset
and approach, as it applied scientiﬁc knowledge and mathematical analysis to
identify and validate optimal solutions. The diagram below describes how the
research was approached, how optimal solutions for each step was found, and the
relationship between the three interdependent main parts in the study.
National Energy System

Figure 3.2: Research structure ﬂow diagram to develop the framework
23
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
The diagram describes how each topic was combined and synthesised step-by-
step. The ﬁrst step in the procedure was to deﬁne the CO2 budget in compliance
with 1.5°C pathway speciﬁed for Denmark.
This budget was the constraint
which was used for modelling the future energy system and scenarios. From the
scenarios, CO2-emission factors were derived from the power-, district heat- and
transportation sectors. These values were dynamically decreasing over time and
related to the scenarios.
They were incorporated as data inputs in both the
framework and as input for embodied carbon. Embodied carbon was assessed
on a range of variations for exterior insulation, interior insulation, windows and
solar panels. LCA’s were made for implementation years ranging from 2018 to
2050, and incorporated CO2-emission factors from the power and transportation
sector exclusively.
The results used the LCI to reassess and determine the
dynamic LCA, where the timing of emissions during the buildings life cycle was
implemented to achieve more accurate results of embodied CO2. For operational
carbon, building case studies were reproduced from data for a residential building
and an oﬃce building. Variations of renovation strategies were employed, and
the energy performance improvements were calculated using design tools. Lastly,
the framework was employed, and the most optimal solutions were investigated
based on CO2 emissions and economic evaluations.

3.4
Energy Systems
3.4.1
CO2-Emissions Budget
The carbon budget estimations used in this study were based on reference values
from IPCC’s report (Rogelj et al., 2018). Their deﬁnition of the remaining CO2-
emissions budget is the estimated cumulative net global CO2-emissions from a
given start date to the time that the CO2-emissions reach net-zero. Their budget
only considers CO2-emissions, which also will be applied in the methods for this
study. Net-zero emissions are reached when the total CO2-emissions equals the
CO2 uptake from, e.g. forests or CCS technologies. They were calculated by
TCRE method (Transient climate response to cumulative CO2 emissions), a ratio
of the average surface temperature response to carbon dioxide emissions using
25
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
the historical warming period of 2006-2015 in the projection. The projection
had thresholds of 1.5°C and 2.0°C degrees of temperature increase above pre-
industrial levels, with the percentiles in Table 3.3 indicating the CO2 emissions
left in the world and the ’chance’ that it would have to remain within this
temperature threshold. The chosen temperature target in this research was based
on limiting the temperature increase to 1.5°C degrees above pre-industrial levels,
with 66% chance that the temperature would stay below that threshold.
Temperature Target

IPCC < 2.0°C

Table 3.3:
Global budget left from 2018 to limit global warming based on
temperature limit and percentage probability to stay under it. This study applied
the global 570 GtCO2 budget
There is still no clear agreement on how to distribute the CO2-emissions budget
between countries (World Bank, 2019). However, there have been suggestions on
using weighting principles of a nation’s current emissions, historical emissions,
population and GDP as a weighting parameter, to distribute the global budget.

Based on the weighting principles, the danish carbon budget can vary between
438 MtCO2 to 2338 MtCO2. The total Danish budget decided for this study
was 500 MtCO2 to prepare the system for a weighting between population and
emissions because this budget yielded an ambitious target. The calculation for
the weighting formula can be seen in appendix C.
The data collected on CO2-emissions was based on historical data from UNFCCC
(2019) database for National Inventory Submissions compiled into a dataset for
territorial emissions by the Global Carbon Atlas (2019). The interval for the
historical accumulation ranged from 1960 to 2017 for every country in the world.
The data collected on the world population was based on oﬃcial demographic
data and was retrieved from The World Bank (2019b). For the GDP dataset, the
same data source was used (The World Bank, 2019a). The dataset retrieved its
data from ’World Bank national accounts data’ and ’OECD National Accounts
data ﬁles’.

3.4.2
Energy Model
The long term planning of an energy system is complex and uncertain. Still,
decision-makers need to optimise the resources and prioritise the necessary
actions to fulﬁl visions of decarbonising the energy system.
The long term
27
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
analysis for the Danish energy system was evaluated with The Integrated
MARKAL-EFOM System (TIMES), which is a model generator used to explore
solutions for future energy scenarios.
It used a bottom-up supply approach
and linear programming to provide an economic objective function for least-
cost technologies that were required to meet the end-user demand within a
constrained low-emission strategy (CO2-budget).

Figure 3.4 gives an overview of the steps conducted for building the TIMES model
and generating the scenarios. The elements will be explained in the following
sections hereafter.
3.4.2.1
Model Speciﬁcations
The model was an optimisation model with full foresight,i.e. ﬁxed start and
endpoint, which meant that the model would decide when and how it wanted
to invest over the entire time horizon. Table 3.5 gives an overview of the main
categories and speciﬁcations used to build the TIMES model.
28
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
Category
Speciﬁcation

- Denmark (Single region)

- 2018 to 2050

- Power & Heat sector
- Residential & Commercial

- Transport sector

Table 3.5: TIMES model speciﬁcations
The energy system of Denmark was modelled as a single-region with only
exogenous imports and exports, which meant that the collection of data was
found at a national level and not separated into smaller areas.
From the base year in 2018 and over the entire time horizon, the years were
separated into eight timeslices to represent situations with, e.g. low renewable
supply and high demands. The seasonal timeslices were thus divided into day and
night to represent, e.g. summer day, summer night, winter day and winter night
etc. The resolution of the timeslices was relatively low to keep the model simple.
It can, however, be discussed if the model should have additional timeslices to
represent more time periods during a year. The deﬁned timeslices can be seen
in Appendix G.
The availability of renewable technologies and electricity demand was estimated
from historical data from monthly reports on electricity supply from the Danish
Energy Agency (DEA). Heating degree days from a ’normal heating year’ was
29
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
used to determine the heat curve.
The model considered all ﬂows between
mining and trading of commodities in the supply sector to the consumption
of energy in the end-user sectors. The transformation technologies, provided in
the model, were based on mature technologies and did not consider technologies
under development. It meant that new technologies only considered price and
eﬃciency developments over time.
3.4.2.2
Reference Energy System
The Reference Energy System (RES) was established to replicate the existing
system in the base year 2018. The RES was based on the energy balance from
2018 retrieved from Eurostat, and it provided an overview of the amount of
energy extracted from domestic sources, traded, transformed and consumed.

Figure 3.5: Reference Energy System for Denmark showing the interconnections
and commodities exchanged between sectors
Figure 3.5 shows the reference energy system and the links between the
sectors.
By nature, the model was a simple representation of the reality as
some mechanisms could not be reproduced.
It was, however, essential to
30
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
demonstrate that the model was a reasonable representation of the actual system
by calibrating it to replicate the energy statistics in the base year.
A simpliﬁed version of the energy balance was made to limit the number of
commodities and processes in the model. Some commodities and processes were
merged when falling under the same category for simpliﬁcation. For example, all
solid fuel commodities were determined as coal. The merged commodities can
be seen in Appendix D. This reworked simpliﬁed version was the reference for
building the base year of the model sector by sector.
Supply
The supply sector consisted of three parts:
Deﬁnition of primary energy
commodities,
domestic mining or exogenous trading processes of energy
commodities, and fuel conversion technologies (reﬁneries). Commodities were
aggregated according to the simpliﬁed energy balance and were either connected
to other sectors or transformed in reﬁneries.
The model consisted of an
oil reﬁnery where crude oil was transformed into petroleum products and
bioreﬁneries, where waste and biomass was converted into biofuels. Technology
data for the reﬁneries were gathered from DEA and Force Technology (Evald
et al., 2013).
Furthermore, the supply part used socio-economic calculations
(Danish Energy Agency, 2019b) for the price projections gathered from DEA
and national statistics on reserves of fossil fuels (Danish Energy Agency, 2018).
For land-use renewable potentials were found on databases from ’IRENA’
(International Renewable Energy Agency, 2018).
Power & Heat
The power & heat sector represented the transformation technologies that
produced electricity and district heat.
It included conventional conversion
plants and renewable technologies which consumed energy commodities provided
from the supply sector.
Conventional conversion plants were divided based
on fuel consumption.
Furthermore, combined heat and power (CHP) plants
were separated between backpressure plants and extraction plants. The DEA
provided data for conventional energy plant’s capacities in their background
report (Danish Energy Agency, 2015) and process speciﬁcation data in their
technology catalogue for electricity and district heat generation (Danish Energy
Agency and Energinet, 2016a). Due to a shortage of data, capacities for heating
plants were determined based on estimations on full-load hours found in the
technology catalogue.
31
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
Residential & Commercial
The residential and commercial sectors behaviour were the main focus in the
model.
The residential sector was modelled in two types:
detached and
multistorey buildings. The commercial buildings included oﬃces, institutions,
shops etc. The sectors were linked to the supply sector by consuming primary
energy resources and power & heat sector for oﬀ-site power and district heat
consumption.

Figure 3.6: Disaggregation overview of the residential and commercial sector
The disaggregation of the sectors was as illustrated in ﬁgure 3.6, where all
building types were divided based on energy labels and fuel technology. The
demands for heating, cooling and electricity was estimated based on data of
building types from Denmarks Statistics (Danmarks Statistik, 2018) and was
disaggregated between energy labels and calculated from ’SparEnergi’ database
(Spar Energi, 2018) by using a weighting principle. The consumption of fuels
between the types and energy labels were distributed based on the energy limits
in the energy label table, which can be seen in Appendix E.1. An overview of
the estimated distribution can be viewed in Appendix F.1 and F.2. Capacities of
heating, cooling and electricity technologies were calibrated to match the energy
balance and the demands in the base year. The technical speciﬁcations were
gathered in the technology catalogue for individual heating from DEA (Danish
Energy Agency and Energinet, 2016b), and these can be seen in Appendix H.4-
H.6
Industry
The industry sector was an aggregation of branches like construction, material
32
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
production, food, ﬁshing and agriculture.
The demands were thus simpliﬁed
to cover space heating, electricity and process heating and were based on IDA
Visions report (Mathiesen et al., 2015).
Process heating was an aggregation
of low-, medium- and high-temperature processes.
It was modelled to follow
forecasts from DEA’s energy scenarios report (Danish Energy Agency, 2014)
to limit the scope of this study. The technical speciﬁcations were gathered in
the technology catalogue for industrial process heat from DEA (Danish Energy
Agency and Energinet, 2020). The technological data can be seen in Appendix
H.7
Transport
The transport sector was also a simpliﬁcation, which covered technologies to meet
passenger kilometres demand and freight demand.
The technologies to carry
passengers covered cars, buses, trains, ships and ﬂights, where freight was met
by ships, trucks and vans. The technologies were modelled to be carbon neutral
either by biofuels or electriﬁcation. Data on eﬃciencies was collected through the
number of vehicles and driven kilometres which were collected from databases
of Eurostat and Odyssee, with the fuel type decided from the energy balance on
transportation. Data and projections for new transportation technologies were
found from publications of NREL (Jadun et al., 2017) and calculation methods
from DEA (Danish Energy Agency, 2016). A break-down of the fuel distribution
for the end-use technologies are found in Appendix F.3, which includes both the
transport sector and the industry sector, where technical data are presented in
Appendix H.8.
3.4.2.3
Future Energy System
The technology catalogues from DEA included outlooks on technical and
economic parameters for selected, representative technologies which were used
in the model. The model was then free to make investments between all deﬁned
technologies that complied with the demands and user constraints. An overview
of technical and economic parameters for processes in all sectors are provided in
appendix H.
3.4.2.4
Demand Projection
The demand projections were highly uncertain but at the same time, crucial as
drivers for the model. They were likely to respond to price changes in energy
33
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
services, but price elasticities were neglected in this study. Diﬀerent assumptions
were made for the end-user sectors:
residential, commercial, industry and
transport.
Residential and Commercial
Both the residential and commercial sector assumed an annual construction rate
for new buildings of 0.5% and a demolishing rate of 0.1%. This rate was derived
from DEA’s outlook (Danish Energy Agency, 2019a).
New structures were
modelled as building type ’A’, whereas existing building types of ’E’, ’F’ and
’G’ were building types that were demolished.
Industry
The demand projection in the industry sector was estimated based on half of the
annual growth rate of the GDP in Denmark projected in the EU28: Reference
Scenario (Capros et al., 2016).
Transport
The demand projection for transportation was also found in the reference EU28:
Reference Scenario (Capros et al., 2016), which included predictions for the
diﬀerent types of traﬃc.
3.4.2.5
Scenario Speciﬁcation
The objective of the model was to investigate the best heat-saving scenario for
the energy system and to derive CO2-factors and roadmaps for buildings. It
was done by modelling three scenarios with diﬀerent magnitudes of heat-saving
potentials. The total heat consumption in the base year was 53.62 TWh which
was reduced with three heat-saving scenarios: A moderate, ambitious and very
ambitious heat-saving strategy.

The heat-saving potentials were based on previous calculations made by SBI in
their report about heat savings in existing buildings (Wittchen et al., 2017). The
report considered the heat-saving potential in the building stock of residential
and commercial buildings that already needed renovation for other purposes.
Based on Pedersen et al. (2019), it was assumed in the model that approximately
70% of the current building mass was to be renovated in 2050. Table 3.6 shows
the results from three scenarios.
All scenarios included acceptable eﬀorts to
maintain minimum requirements for the structures and indoor climate.
The
moderate scenario corresponded to insulating empty cavity walls. The ambitious
scenario added some insulation of the roofs and ceilings and also replaced old
windows with energy label ’A’ windows. The last and most ambitious scenario
corresponded to all mentioned actions and added full insulation of roofs and
ceilings and also full focus on energy reductions on renovated parts in the
building envelope. The general calculations for the heat-saving potentials and
the distribution between residential detached and multistorey buildings and
commercial building along with the energy labels can be seen in Appendix I.

Figure 3.7: Illustration of the modelled heat-saving potential. Energy labels with
’D’ or lower can ’quickly’ raise the energy label with two levels, whereas the rest
needs extensive renovations. The right side shows the corresponding estimated
annualised renovation prices for each label and renovation type
The price estimations for energy investments was also based on calculations from
Wittchen et al. (2017). Figure 3.7 illustrates how the potentials were modelled
and that it was more cost-eﬃcient to renovate buildings with low energy labels.
It was assumed that buildings with energy labels ’D’ or lower could, at a lower
cost, increase their energy label two levels. Better energy labels were considered
more expensive and had less potential for heat-savings, but because it is assumed
that 70% of the current building mass needed renovation, it is estimated that a
35
==PAGE:52==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
small volume of the current ’A’ buildings can be improved in the future. The
model does not consider more strict energy labels so that the best energy label
corresponds to ’A+’ throughout the time horizon.
The model assumes that
all energy investments have a lifetime of 50 years, which are considered when
implementing annualised renovation costs.
3.4.2.6
Model Assumptions
The model was set up with constraints of Denmark’s climate goals and obligations
which was based on the Energy Agreement struck in 2018. For instance, Denmark
has a target to become fossil-free in 2050. These data were incorporated in the
model to ’overrule’ decisions for the development.

Figure 3.8 shows the years and initiatives incorporated into the scenarios of the

The taxes on

create incentives for, e.g. use of heat pumps. Production of electricity by coal
consumption was phased out in 2030.

Table 3.7: Renewable energy potentials
There were some space restrictions related to the investments of renewable
technologies such as wind and solar energy.
The ’Energy Agreement 2018’
states that the number of onshore wind turbines was to be halved in 2030. It
was, however, assumed that the capacity would increase slightly due to larger
and more eﬃcient turbines. Estimations on applicable roof area for buildings
were found to be 15,000 hectares. These were divided between residential and
commercial sectors to calculate the corresponding capacity for solar energy. It
was assumed that any barriers for implementation of other technologies would
be eliminated, such as space requirements for expanding transmission grids.
Other user constraints in the model were deﬁned to keep the model behaviour
realistic, such that the implementation of new technology did not happen all at
once. The CO2 values for fossil commodities were derived from Danish Energy
Agency (2019b), and were deﬁned as zero for biomass so it could be implemented
and deﬁned as a part of the net-zero system in 2050.
However, the carbon-
neutrality of biomass are questioned, which means that other strategies might
be considered in reality. This will be elaborated later in the discussion chapter.
The TIMES model, furthermore, assumed a perfectly competitive market and
full foresight, which extended to the entire time horizon.
37
==PAGE:54==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.4.2.7
Deriving Results From the Model
CO2-factor
The three heat-saving scenarios provided diﬀerent behaviours in the energy
system, which aﬀected the CO2-factors. The factors were found for power and
district heat generation and deﬁned as CO2-emissions per sold kWh energy.
CO2-emissions between electricity and heat were evaluated between the four
methods: energy content, 125%-method, 200%-method and energy quality (see
Appendix B for equations). The DEA recommends the 125 %-method, which
was the reference method used for later calculations.
The sold energy was
calculated as the total energy consumed in the residential, commercial, industry
and transport sector, and thus the CO2-factor considered grid-losses. CO2-factors
were furthermore found for the transportation sector and incorporated in the
embodied CO2 life cycle assessment of renovation strategies.
Due to modelling purposes, TIMES did not directly account for renewable
technologies’ relative contribution to CO2 throughout its life cycle.
CO2
emissions released in phases from construction, operation and decommissioning
were therefore added manually to the CO2-factors.
The values applied can be seen in table 3.8 and were diﬀerentiated for each
generation type:
Generation type
kg CO2/kWh
Solar PV
0.097
Wind
0.030
Biomass & Waste
0.052
Table 3.8: LCA for each generation type. Adapted from WNA (2011)
Emission Roadmaps
The electricity and district heat consumption for each building type was
converted to CO2 emissions by using the calculated CO2-factors. It was added
with emissions from individual boilers and emissions related to oil reﬁneries in
the supply sector for all building types to include both direct and indirect CO2
emissions for each building type.
38
==PAGE:55==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.5
Embodied Carbon
3.5.1
Renovation strategies
Assessment of embodied carbon was based on the materials and components
used for renovation strategies, to mitigate CO2 through energy optimisation of
building operation. Variations of renovation strategies were assessed for both
virgin and circular materials for enhancing the building envelope through exterior
insulation of facade and interior insulation of the outer wall. Another strategy
assessed was a substitution to more energy-eﬃcient windows, while the last
strategy assessed was to implement on-site renewable production through solar
PV panels on roofs.
The renovation strategies are visualised in the diagram
below.
PROJECT
EXTERIOR INSULATION
INTERIOR INSULATION
WINDOWS
SOLAR PANELS
Figure 3.9: Hand drawn conceptual diagram of renovation strategies assessed.
Inspired by Dansk Arkitektur Center (2011)
The main idea of the embodied carbon assessment was to compare the strategies’
total eﬀect with the operational phase to assess the consequential reduction.
Each strategy on an embodied level would optimise the energy consumption but
would require an investment of CO2.
The CO2 investment, was assessed for 27 renovation variations in total. These
renovation variations all had 33 diﬀerent implementation years, with the building
life cycle phases of the materials decreasing each year due to substitution of
fossil fuels to renewables in the energy system. This led to 891 unique dynamic
variations of renovation strategies.

and one type of plaster. Interior insulation had two types of interior layers of
gypsum. Each of these types had diﬀerent levels of thickness in insulation. For
windows, two types were assessed, while for onsite solar PV panels, bundles of
12, 24 and 48 panels were assessed. The overview can be seen below:

3.5.3
Life Cycle Assessment
The Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) method was used for the research to deﬁne
the environmental impact of renovation strategies for circular and conventional
building materials and components. The LCA’s applied the standardised method
described in EN 15978 (2011). The modules that were included in the assessment
were A1-A3, A4-A5, B2-B4 and C2-C4 as illustrated in the ﬁgure below:
Product

Construction

Figure 3.11: Life cycle phases for buildings and building materials according to
EN 15978: 2011. Adapted from Birgisdottir et al. (2017)
B1 ’Use’ was left out as it was assumed that there was a minimal environmental
impact from the normal use of building products (excluding those related to
energy) for all strategies.
B5 ’Refurbishment’ was not necessary after the
implementation of strategies and consequently assumed to be ’0’. B6 ’Operational
energy usage’ and B7 ’Operational water usage’ were left out as they were
accounted for in the operational carbon study, while C1 ’Deconstruction’ was
not deemed necessary for the strategies. Module D were the beneﬁts beyond the
system boundary and were left out in the method described by EN 15978. This
was also preferred in this research, to give full beneﬁts for the second user of the
materials instead of the recycler (ﬁrst user). As the strategies were connected to
building extensions, materials ’removed’, were not considered as they were seen
as having a minor impact. For windows, it was assumed that ’old’ windows were
either reused or recycled and thus the beneﬁtter would account for transportation
of the windows and reprocessing as deﬁned by the system boundary.
The strategies for exterior insulation, interior insulation and windows strategies
had the functional unit 1 m2.
The functional unit for solar PV panels was
assessed on 1 panel (of 1.5 m2).
41
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.5.4
Data and Analysis
The analysis of variations was based on data from suppliers EPD’s and from
the German database for building products Ökobau.dat. They were assessed
by using ecoinvent’s LCI database from the software SimaPro.
For the LCI,
the cut-oﬀallocation approach was chosen as the system boundary, so all reused
materials were ’burden-free’ in its ’secondary’ life cycle and accounted for entirely
in its primary life cycle.
This approach was chosen to avoid accounting for
the emissions twice, as historical emissions were already accounted for in the
scenarios. To calculate the CO2-emissions, a new method was deﬁned in SimaPro
adapted from the method IPCC 2013 GWP 100a. This method recalculated 106
substances of emissions to ’kg CO2-equivalent’ in the time frame of 100 years from
the renovation in 2018. Because the research was interested in the impact of ’kg
CO2-emissions’ exclusively, only four carbon dioxide substances were chosen in
the reinterpreted method to align with the emissions budget from the energy
systems model.
It was deemed that the CO2-eq from other substances were
minor.
The LCA’s were further recalculated to improve the accuracy of static aggregated
emissions resulting from all emissions throughout the life cycle to be released at
the same time.
Instead, the LCI’s were found at each stage to account for
the timing, as releasing large amounts of emissions would not yield the same
environmental impact as the same amount in smaller emission pulses spread
over several years. For calculating the dynamic life cycle assessment (DLCA),
the research followed Levasseurs’ methodology (Levasseur et al., 2010).
The
method used the Bern carbon cycle-climate model and calculated the cumulative
radiative forcing per unit mass of CO2 for each emission pulse. The method
applied the radiative forcing concept from GWP to recalculate all pulse emissions
happening through a time frame reverted back to time 0. All strategies were
calculated using a self-developed tool for DLCA as illustrated in Figure 3.12.
Pulse emissions were pre-determined to be released in the following years:
Building type

3.5.5
Electricity and Transportation
The CO2-factors were derived from the energy system scenario modelling and
applied on each implementation year from 2018-2050 to calculate the 33 unique
LCA’s by assuming that manufacturing and production were performed the
same year as implementation.
For electricity, the fuel mix was measured in
’kg CO2/kWh’, and for transportation, the truck-fuel mix was measured in
’kg CO2/tkm’.
The main contribution of electricity data was deducted from
the LCA’s and manually calculated for the time-related dynamics of CO2 from
electricity. This meant that all sub-life cycles remained constant and were not
subject to dynamic factors. The transportation was added manually and was
assumed to be the same for all strategies. It was assumed that there was 50 km
involved in the product and use stage, and furthermore 25 km for the construction
and end of life stage. The LCI (including impact calculations) for all materials,
including assumptions, can be seen in Appendix K.
3.5.6
Addressing Lifetime and Resource Quantity
The lifetime of strategies was derived from Aagard et al. (2013) and was based
on a resource consumption quantity principle. In cases when the strategies had
a shorter lifetime than the average lifetime left of the building, the resource
quantity had to be multiplied in the LCA. For simpliﬁcation purposes, the
research did not distinguish between building materials but instead focused on
building components as a whole. The resource quantity for each strategy can be
seen below with the example showing the resource factor from year 2018:

3.6
Operational Carbon
3.6.1
Case Studies
The research on operational carbon assessed two existing buildings.
The
buildings were a commercial and a residential building.
Operational energy
was ﬁrst calculated on the existing buildings. This was done to measure the
energy consumption of ’status quo’ until a strategy was implemented and the
energy reduced. Afterwards, operational carbon was calculated before and after
applying the diﬀerent strategies with the framework.
Figure 3.13 gives an
overview of the buildings that were examined. Case 1 was an educational/oﬃce
building at DTU Campus. It was deﬁned as a commercial building. Case 2 was
a building that was primarily used for residential purposes, with a small area
used for commercial purposes. According to ’The Danish Building Code’, the
building was categorised as a residential building because the commercial area
covered less than 20% of the total area.

3.6.2
Operational Energy
The buildings operational energy was calculated using, Rockwool Energy Design,
which complies with Danish building regulations and national standards. The
data applied for the research was gathered from the owner’s administrators of
the buildings.
The data used for the energy calculations were derived from,
architectural drawings, building descriptions, 3D models, inspection reports and
the buildings real-time energy consumption in full years from 2016 to 2019 see
table 3.11.
The existing operational energy was derived by using the data
collected and calculating the energy demand used for the categories, space
heating, domestic hot water and electricity for building installations. The energy
demand for the categories was calculated according to the principles in the Danish
Building Code (2018). The energy demand used for appliances was not accounted
for in both cases, but the energy used for lighting was accounted for in Case 1.
In some cases where there was a gap in the gathered data, qualiﬁed assumptions
were made based on standard building practices from the construction year of
the buildings. Assumptions will be further elaborated when describing the data
entry in the next sections.

==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.6.3
Rockwool Energy Design
Figure 3.14: Interface of Rockwool Energy Design
The operational energy was calculated by using the software tool Rockwool
Energy Design, developed by ROCKWOOL group.
It calculates a building’s
energy consumption for a year in compliance with SBI 213, DS418 and
DS/ENISO 13788. The ﬁnal energy demand was given in ’kWh/m2’ and was
multiplied with an energy factor.
The used energy factor for electricity and
district heat was 1.9 and 0.85, respectively (The Danish Construction et al.,
2020). These factors were deﬁned by The Danish Building Code, and are adapted
from the Danish Energy Agency to express the eﬃciency of electricity, district
heat or other forms of heat. The factor was furthermore applied in the framework
to express the energy demand, and the factors were assumed to be constant
throughout the time period.
Rockwool Energy Design works by ﬁlling out following parameters: Building
envelope, ventilation, internal heat gains, DHW, lighting (Only Case 1),
installations and energy supply.
47
==PAGE:64==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
3.6.3.1
Building Envelope
The building envelope consists of outer walls, roof, ground slab, windows and
doors. Through these building components, the transmission of heat and line
losses occurs. The thermal transmittance, known as the U-value, was determined
for each building component using Rockwool U-value calculator, which is an
integrated tool in Rockwool Energy Design.
The tool was used to calculate
existing building’s components and the renovation strategies based on data for
the building components thermal conductivity and thickness. The values applied
are shown in Table 3.12.

drawings (Appendix Q and R).
3.6.3.4
Internal Heat Gains
Internal heat gains include heat from occupants and electric appliances. Internal
heat gain from people was assumed to be 1.5 W/m2 for residential and 4 W/m2 for
commercial. Heat gain for appliances was assumed to be 3.5 W/m2 for residential
and 6 W/m2 for commercial. These values were applied for both cases, with the
standard values obtained according to SBI 213 (2005).
