3.6.3.6
Energy Supply
In both cases, the heating demand was covered by radiators connected to the
district heating grid. The internal heat distribution between supply and radiators
was related to heat losses through the piping system. The pipe material, size
and insulation thickness was assumed for both cases to be 1 1/2" steel pipes with
30 mm insulation. The length of the pipes was measured from the architectural
drawings (Appendix Q and R). Case 1 was 898.5 m and Case 2 was 262 m. The
eﬀect from the heat exchanger was assumed to 92 kW and 144 kW for Case 1
and 2, respectively.
3.6.4
Energy-Saving Initiatives
Diﬀerent energy-saving strategies were planned on the existing buildings, and
included exterior insulation, interior insulation, windows and establishment of
PV-panels on the roof. All strategies were applied in both cases as a preliminary
assessment of CO2 embodied in renovation strategies. The strategies were based
on standard energy renovation practices and did not go into detail about how each
implementation would aﬀect local plans or the detailed information on building
and assembling of the strategies.
The energy speciﬁcation data applied for the strategies of exterior and interior
insulation can be seen in Table 3.18 and 3.19. The data for windows can be seen
in Table 3.20 while the speciﬁcations for solar panels can be seen in Table 3.21.
Cladding type

3.6.5
CO2 Framework Tool
A framework was developed to measure the holistic eﬀect of the three researched
topics, and consequently, the aggregated CO2 consumption of buildings in the
53
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
analysed period 2018-2050. The framework had a limit value for each building
type which indicated how much CO2 the building could consume to meet the
1.5°C target and whether buildings had to apply energy-saving strategies to
reduce its operational CO2 consumption for the remaining years by investing
on embodied CO2.
The tool worked by multiplying the electricity and heat
consumption with the corresponding CO2 factor for each year which was derived
from the energy system scenarios. Each building type was constrained by a limit
value that was incorporated in the framework (Appendix O.1-O.3).
The following ﬁgure shows input area for the self-developed framework tool.

3.6.6
Price Estimates of Renovation Strategies
Prices for each renovation were furthermore compared for the best strategies to
understand the ﬁnancial aspect of reducing CO2. The price estimates for each
strategy was assessed using Sigma Estimates. The cost for labour and material
was taken from the Sigma Estimates built-in-library database for carpenters,
bricklayer and painter and was excluding VAT. In the cases where the material
was not available, the cost was found from the supplier, and the VAT was
54
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 3. METHODS & MATERIALS
deducted. The libraries in Sigma Estimates were based on Molio and Exact’s
price database. All the prices for the strategies were calculated in DKK/m2.
The
material
cost
for
the
windows
was
found
from
a
supplier
’Vinduegrossisten.dk’ with the labour cost derived from Sigma Estimates.
Case 1 had two types of window sizes, while Case 2 had eight diﬀerent window
sizes.
The cost of a solar PV-panel system was found from supplier ’Vivaenergi’. Since
there was no PV-system with the same kWp as the ones used in the energy
renovations, the price for the energy renovation and PV-systems was found by
interpolating the cost for two systems from the supplier.
All prices for renovation strategies can be seen in detailed in Appendix P.

The following chapter presents the results from the individual research ﬁndings.
First, Section 4.1 ’Energy System’ presents the development of energy for
sectors in Denmark, their technological development and corresponding CO2
roadmaps and CO2-factors. Secondly, Section 4.2 ’Embodied Carbon’ presents
the renovation strategies’ embodied CO2 from the inﬂuence of dynamic factors on
LCA. Thirdly, Section 4.3 ’Operational Energy’ presents the existing case studies’
current energy consumption and the energy savings achieved by implementing
each renovation strategy.
Lastly, the chapter provides a synthesis overview of the individual results and
how they will be applied to the framework in the following chapter.
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
4.1
Energy System
This section provides the modelling results and illustrates the development of
how the Danish energy system becomes carbon neutral in 2050 by providing
100% renewable sources while at the same time not exceeding the CO2-budget
constraint in the time horizon 2018-2050. The section starts by analysing the
energy consumption in the system and then relates the energy-use to carbon
emissions to ultimately provide the CO2-factors and roadmaps, which is applied
in the CO2-framework.
The result section is based on values from the ’very ambitious’ scenario because
this showed the least average annual total system costs of approximately 108
billion DKK. General results from the other scenarios will be used in the
discussion chapter.
Furthermore, it uses the 125% CO2 allocation method for CO2-factors.
The
following sections will not go into depth with the economic aspect of the system
as it will be elaborated in the discussion. Instead, the results will focus on the
technical perspective.
4.1.1
System Overview
0

Figure 4.1: Direct energy consumption in end-use demand sectors for Denmark

58
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
The results from the model show that the direct end-use energy consumption is
subject to a decrease towards 2050, even with external factors such as population
and welfare increasing and leading to higher demands for, e.g. buildings, vehicles
and general consumption. Figure 4.1 shows that the ﬁnal energy consumption
declines for all end-use sectors apart from the industry. The main reasons for
the lower energy consumption is due to increasing energy-eﬃcient technologies
and heat saving initiatives. Transportation is the most energy-heavy sector and
Figure 4.2 furthermore underlines this as it shows that the sector has the most
signiﬁcant environmental impact because of the high CO2-emissions related to
its combustion phase.

CO2-reduction vs. 1990
Figure 4.2: Direct and indirect end-use CO2-emissions for Denmark in MtCO2
including a percentage CO2-reduction compared to 1990 emissions level per year
The black line in Figure 4.2 illustrates the CO2 reduction compared to its 1990
emission level in the energy system. The Figure shows that the total CO2 level in
2018 is around 36 Mt CO2 which corresponds to a reduction of 27% compared to
the reference level. This amount serves as the starting point for the model. The
target of 70% CO2 reduction of the reference level is met in 2030 (corresponding
to the political constraints) and the CO2 emissions are reduced by 100% CO2 in
2050 and thus the system becomes carbon neutral.
All indirect CO2 emissions from the supply and power & heat sector are assigned
to the respective end-use sectors, where the energy is consumed, to include all
CO2 emissions. The following sections break down the energy development in
each sector of the system.
59
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
4.1.1.1
Power & Heat Sector
The power & heat sector undergoes a quick transition by out-phasing fossil fuels
and substituting with renewables.
For power generation, wind energy gets a
dominant role in the sector with electricity expanding rapidly towards 2035
by heavy investments in primarily oﬀshore wind farms. The investments make
Denmark more self-suﬃcient with lower electricity imports and making revenues
on exports to exogenous regions, which partly explains the expanding production.
0

TWh/year

Onshore Wind
Offshore Wind
Refineries
Figure 4.3: Oﬀ-site electricity production for Denmark in TWh per year
Figure 4.3 incorporates the initiatives from the ’Energy Agreement 2018’ with the
phase-out of coal-ﬁred power generation, implementation of three new oﬀshore
wind farms and the ambition of reducing the number of onshore wind turbines
by half. The constraint on onshore wind keeps their production steady as only
new turbines are implemented when existing are decommissioned. However, the
production increases over time as the technology gets more eﬃcient. Solar PV
receives a more signiﬁcant role in the future system as the technology will create
more ﬂexibility and variance on availability periods and thus stabilise a heavily
dominant wind production in the power system. Furthermore, a small share of
conventional power production, from biomass and renewable waste is also applied
in the energy system to provide electricity when the ﬂuctuating technologies are
insuﬃcient.

Figure 4.4: District heat production for Denmark in TWh per year
Generally, the total district heat production experiences a slight increase over the
time horizon illustrated by Figure 4.4. The ﬁgure shows that coal-ﬁred plants are
phased out for heat generation and are replaced with other technologies by 2030.
Gas plants, another heavy CO2-emitter, continues until the power & heat sector
is carbon neutral in 2040. The ﬁgure furthermore shows that large-size heat
pumps will cover around half of the district heat production in 2050, which also
explains some of the expansion in power generating units. Other technologies,
such as biomass will have a smaller contribution to the heat generation in 2050
as other renewable technologies, such as solar heating and geothermal, will
increase their capacities. As there will be a higher production of biofuels for
the transport sector, some of the excess heat from the reﬁneries are used for the
heating system, with excess heat available for district heat production. Both
the electricity and district heat production increases over time, while the end-
use energy consumption decreases. This suggests that the future system relies
heavily on oﬀ-site energy production. The energy consumption in the end-use
sectors are shown in the following sections, where especially the industry sector
increases its consumption of district heat and electricity.
4.1.1.2
Residential Sector
The overall development of fuel consumption in residential buildings is illustrated
in Figure 4.5.
It includes fuels for heating technologies and electricity for
appliances. The results show that the overall energy consumption will decrease
61
==PAGE:78==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
towards 2050, which is primarily due to heat savings. The study did not account
for electricity savings for appliances, which is seen in the increase in electricity
consumption. Electricity will thus account for 37% in 2050 compared to 19%
in 2018.
Approximately 45% of the electricity consumption in 2050 will be
supplied by roof installed solar PV, which releases some pressure from the oﬀ-
site electricity generation in the power & heat sector. The electricity for heating
will also have a slight increase, which is caused by an increase in individual
heat pumps. The heat pumps have signiﬁcantly higher eﬃciency compared to
other heating technologies, which means that the modest electric consumption
produces relatively more heat.

Figure 4.5:
Fuel consumption for electricity and heating demands in the
residential sector for Denmark in TWh per year
The space heating and domestic hot water demand are covered either by
individual boilers or district heat.
Buildings inside the district heating grids
correspond to 46% of the fuel consumption for heating in residential buildings in
2018. In 2050, this share is 78% which suggests that the grid requires expansions
to fulﬁl the heat demand in the future. The remaining buildings are dependant
on individual boilers, which includes the CO2 heavy fossil fuels, oil and gas.
These boilers will be phased out in 2030 and 2040, respectively, which means the
operational energy in the residential sector is carbon neutral in 2040.
The replacement of old oil and gas boilers includes implementation of individual
heat pumps. They will be favoured as electricity taxes are relaxed towards 2030
and are in sync with the cleaner production of electricity.
62
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
As multistorey buildings generally are assumed located in urbanised areas,
district heating covers around 90% of their heating demand throughout the time
horizon. Detached buildings have a higher share of buildings outside the district
heating grid, which makes them more reliable on individual heating.
Their
carbon-neutral options include solar heating, heat pumps and wood heating
units, with the latter being the only decreasing technology for both detached
and multistorey houses.
The disaggregated results for heating consumption for detached and multistorey
residential buildings can be examined in Appendix J.1 and J.2.
4.1.1.3
Commercial Sector
Similar to residential buildings, the heat-saving initiative causes the overall
energy consumption to decrease, despite increasing electricity consumption.
Figure 4.6 illustrates a prompt decrease in electricity consumption for heating
between 2018 and 2020, which corresponds to a quick replacement of traditional
electric heaters.
Afterwards, a slight increase is noticed which accounts for
implementation of individual heat pumps which beneﬁts, of its high electricity-
to-heat eﬃciency.
Solar PV, installed on the roofs of commercial buildings,
generates on-site power to cover 35% of the commercial electricity demands in
2050.

63
==PAGE:80==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
The district heat expands rapidly towards 2020 as a consequence of the prompt
out-phasing of traditional electric heaters. It can be discussed how realistic this
is in a real system, as many electric heaters probably will be out of reach in the
district heating grid and more likely to be replaced by heat pumps. The use of
biomass for heating will increase towards 2030 and afterwards decrease to only
cover 0.7% of the fuel consumption in 2050.
Oil and gas boilers follow the same trend as illustrated for residential buildings,
which means that the operational energy in the commercial sector is carbon-
neutral in 2040. The heat produced in the commercial buildings can be seen
in Appendix J.3, which shows that district heating will cover 81% of the heat
demand in 2050.
The carbon-neutral results for individual heating in 2050
includes 11% heat pumps, 7% solar heating and 1% wood heating units.
4.1.1.4
Industry Sector
The transition towards carbon-neutrality in the industry sector is more
challenging compared to the previous sectors because of increasing demands
on medium and high temperature processes, which requires fossil fuels. This
is highlighted by Figure 4.7, which shows the overall fuel consumption.
The
industry sector uses a large amount of coal, gas and oil and opposed to the other
sectors, the commodities are only ﬁrst completely phased out in 2050.

Gas boilers are needed for process heating in 2050, which means that the carbon-

The
shift towards renewable and clean energy consumption for process heating in
the sector shifts to heat pumps to cover the low-temperature process demand.
The need for district heat will also increase to support the demand for medium-
temperature processes. The combustion of biomass and renewable waste is used
as carbon-neutral fuels for high-temperature processes along with electric heaters.
The process heating results and trends are assumed to be related to embodied
emissions, which is elaborated in Section 4.1.3.
The fuels that are not consumed by process heating technologies are consumed
to provide heat for the SH and DHW demand, and can be viewed in Appendix

4.1.1.5
Transport Sector
Figure 4.9 shows that the overall energy consumption in the transport
sector decreases towards 2050, which is due to eﬃciency improvements for
transportation technologies, meaning more milage per consumed fuel. Especially
electric vehicle has a high eﬃciency and will be a signiﬁcant part of road
transportation in the future system.
Electrolysis for hydrogen and its
corresponding processes are not considered as an option in the study, which
means that the sector neglects a possible energy-demanding technology.
65
==PAGE:82==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
Reaching net-zero emissions for the transportation sector is also very challenging,
similar to the industry sector. There are substantial investment costs related
to the replacement of the vehicle stock and implementation of bioreﬁneries.
Furthermore, the increasing number of electric vehicles comes with more
investments in charging stations which is not evaluated in the study. The
implementation of biofuels and electricity will make the transport sector carbon-
neutral in 2050, where most of the road transport is fueled with power.
In
contrast, the more heavy transport technologies, such as aviation and shipping,
relies on biofuels.

4.1.2
Energy Savings
The results from the energy savings show the renovation strategies implemented
by the buildings on a national scale. Renovation strategies include heat savings
and implementation of solar PV on the roof.
The heat savings for the ’very ambitious’ scenario for all buildings in the
residential and commercial sector corresponds to almost 21 TWh per year in
2050.
This section will only highlight the results of multistorey residential and
commercial buildings since these types will be evaluated later in the framework.
The heat savings in multistorey residential and commercial buildings correspond
to 42 % of the total heat savings at 8.85 TWh per year in 2050, while the

remaining 58% corresponds to the heat savings in detached residential buildings.
The heat-saving results for detached buildings can be viewed in Appendix J.5.

assumptions for the embodied are presented here.

sector develops towards net-zero emissions. The factors are illustrated below:

(b) Heat CO2-factor
Figure 4.14: CO2-factors derived from the power & heat sector
The results in Figure 4.14 includes the operational CO2-factors, which relate to
the initiatives made in the power & heat sector. The considerable investments
in renewable energy and the phasing out of fossil fuels is the reason for
the decreasing operational CO2-factors.
The results indicate that the power
generating technologies almost reaches net-zero operational emissions in 2030,
with only a slight decrease afterwards until 2040. The operational CO2-factor
for heat will drop more steadily compared to power. The increasing use of heat
pumps makes the CO2-factor more related to the CO2-factor for electricity. The
following years after 2030 also shows a slighter decrease for the CO2-factor until
it reaches zero for operational emissions in 2040 highlighted by the ﬁgure.
However, the new investments also come with embodied emissions, and are
illustrated as blue bars in Figure 4.14.The eﬀect of the embodied neutralisation
factor, from Figure 4.12, can be seen decrease towards 2050. The reduction is
less signiﬁcant compared to the operational emissions, which means that the
embodied CO2-emissions will have an increasingly critical role, contributing to a
100% from 2040 until it reaches 0% in 2050.

Figure 4.15: CO2 roadmap for multistorey residential and commercial buildings
divided in energy labels in kg CO2 per m2
The conclusive eﬀects on the building sector are shown in Figure 4.15. Again,
the results are shown for the building types used in the framework, which means
that results for detached buildings are found in Appendix J.6.
The ﬁgures, displaying the CO2 roadmaps, contain the CO2-intensity per m2
for each building type, throughout the time horizon. The results are related to
all direct emissions from individual heating boilers, and indirect emissions from
power and district heat generation and emissions related to the oil reﬁneries.
Furthermore, embodied emissions are also included to represent the climate
impacts from production, transportation and end-of-life emissions. The sum of
these over the building mass of each type is illustrated in the ﬁgures. As buildings
with low energy labels consume more energy per m2, these emit CO2 accordingly
for both sectors. In general, it is noticed that commercial buildings are most
CO2-intensive since, e.g. hospitals and data centres are very energy demanding.
As for the residential sector, multistorey buildings usually have fewer m2’s per
person, which explains a higher CO2-intensity compared to detached buildings.
All building types require prompt CO2 reductions until 2040, where the
operational energy becomes carbon neutral.
Embodied emissions, from the
production of materials for buildings and energy plants, are thus the only
occurring environmental impact from 2040 but decreases in correspondence with
the carbon neutralisation factor.

4.2.1
Renovation Strategies
To simplify the results in the following chapters, each renovation strategy is
shortened and given an abbreviation. The numbers in the names represent the
variations in insulation thickness or panel units. The symbol ’/’ after each name,
represents when a strategy is combined (e.g. when a renovation of an outer wall
consists of part brick-cladding and part wood-cladding).
The symbol ’+’ in
the name represents when there is an additional strategy (e.g. outer wall and
a window renovation). The symbols apply in the result sections of embodied
carbon, operational carbon and the framework in the ﬁgures (graphs.) The table
below represents an overview of the new names.

Figure 4.16: Static LCA of renovation strategies. Impact from diﬀerent building
phases. Based on cut-oﬀallocation method from database ecoinvent (2014)
The base derivation results from SimaPro are static aggregated results from all
building stages and are based on a fuel mix for the Danish energy system from
2014. The results generally show, that most of the environmental impact stems
from the initial phases A1-A5. However, a large amount of CO2 is stored in
strategies consisting of wood-related products, where the stored CO2 is released
in C1-C4 during incineration.
It is assumed that almost the whole amount
of CO2 released in C1-C4 is absorbed again through the concept of CO2 sink,
related to planting new trees and is therefore modelled as an equilibrium between
incineration and absorption of CO2.
The base derivation also shows that reused strategies (with a minor impact from
dynamic eﬀects not considered) have a lower CO2 impact than conventional
strategies. For example, reused brick cladding strategy is 53-59% lower compared
to its conventional form due to the reduction of impact in the production of
bricks alone, when both strategies have the same insulation thicknesses for both
cases. For reused and conventional timber cladding strategies, the decrease is
approximately 5-8%, whereas the decrease from conventional to reused interior
gypsum is 25-27%. There is a clear correlation between environmental impact
and the type of exterior cladding.
Timber cladding, including maintenance,
73
==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
has the lowest CO2 impact of all exterior strategies regardless of whether it is
reused or conventional. This is followed by reused brick shells, reused bricks,
conventional plaster and conventional bricks when comparing strategies with
the same insulation thickness. The results furthermore show that the type of
materials is pivotal when choosing the best strategy, however, when comparing
the individual material, reused materials are always better as it has almost no
impact in production. Solar Panel PV’s of 1.5 m2 (per panel) is an almost linear
for 12, 24 and 48 panels with only its inverter creating (minimal) deviation.
Figure 4.17 shows the CO2 for each renovation strategy, by its ’base contribution’
and contribution through transportation and electricity from its life cycle phases.

Figure 4.17: Static LCA of renovation strategies. Impact from electricity and
transportation (2014). All renovation strategies LCI can be seen in Appendix K
The base contribution seen in the ﬁgure has the largest share in CO2 for all
strategies and is not subject to dynamic factors from the energy system. The
contribution from transportation and electricity is, however, subject to dynamic
factors from the energy system and therefore, this contribution changes through
the time horizon 2018-2050. The DLCA method is applied to all contributions at
the time of implementation. The resource consumption is applied after, in order
to address the building components lifetime in relation to the building’s lifetime
’left’ (explained in the methodology).
The development of LCA, therefore,
considers dynamic factors from the energy system, application of DLCA eﬀects
and lastly application of resource consumption factor.

For all LCA results, Case 1 generally has a higher impact than Case 2 as it
has a shorter lifespan (45 years compared to 58 years) and thus conﬁrming that
releasing the same amount of emissions spread over several years would cause
less impact than the same amount compressed in less years.

rest of the time horizon. This is due to the energy system ’developing’ most
in terms of transitioning to renewable energy in this period. Table 4.2 shows

to the last year of implementation. It can be seen that the CO2 value drops
from the starting year until the end of the implementation year. For the full

4.2.4
Exterior Insulation Strategies
The results from table 4.2 shows that conventional brick cladding has the highest
impact of all exterior strategies and decreases in the assessed time horizon with
12-14% (dependent on the case study and insulation thickness). Reused timber
76
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
cladding has the lowest impact and decreases less in the time horizon with 10-11%
over time with 120 mm and 150 mm insulation, whereas with 80 mm insulation
it decreases with 15%. This decrease highlights that reused timber increases its
importance, with less insulation, as it yields a higher decrease. The rest of the
assessed exterior strategies can be seen in detail in Appendix M.1 - M.4.
There is a clear indication from the results from 2018-2030 the marginal decrease
is highest speciﬁcally for conventional and reused masonry bricks. This is because
both strategies have a high electricity and transportation share, which are subject
to increasing renewables. When adding all exterior insulation strategies with
varied insulation thicknesses, there are overlaps between each strategy where
some items become better than others in the time horizon. This can be seen
in Appendix M.4 - M.5. The comparison of all exterior strategies, regardless of
insulation thicknesses, cannot be compared through the results alone and needs
a holistic assessment using the framework.
4.2.5
Interior Insulation Strategies
Interior insulation strategies for conventional and reused gypsum can be seen in
detail in Appendix M.7 - M.8. The results show that reused interior gypsum has
a lower impact regardless of the insulation thickness. Each strategy decreases
in the time horizon with the conventional strategy decreasing with 17-18% and
the reused strategy decreasing with 20-21%. The results furthermore show the
highest marginal decrease from 2018 to 2030. The results highlight that gypsum
has a low embodied CO2 when comparing it to other cladding materials. The
downside of gypsum is, however, that it can only work as an indoor material.
4.2.6
Window Strategies
Exterior and interior insulation strategies are not subject to the multiple resource
consumption factor as both strategies have a lifespan of 80 years, which is more
than the average lifespan left for each building case study.
For the window
strategies, multiple resource consumption is required as the lifespan of the
windows are 50 years.
Multiple windows are therefore required to cover the
lifetime of Case 2, which has 58 years remaining. The detailed LCA’s of double
and triple glazing windows can be seen in Appendix M.9 and M.10. The ﬁgure
below shows the window strategies for both cases.

The results from the window strategies shows a diﬀerent pattern compared to

the window strategies, the resource consumption is shown to aﬀect, Case 2, which
starts with a higher impact until the implementation year 2026 when multiple
resource consumption is not required anymore. Also, for the window strategies,
the highest marginal decrease happens between 2018-2032. The results show
that Case 1 decreases with 16-19%, and Case 2 decreases with 27-30% in the
time horizon.
4.2.7

for each bundle can be seen in Appendix M.11 - M.12. The results for solar panel
strategies are also subject to multiple resource consumption as each panel has a
lifespan of 30 years. This means that for Case 2 in the implementation year 2018
the resource consumption factor is almost ’2’ as the building lifetime left is 58
years from 2018. For Case 1, the resource consumption is ’1.5’ in 2018, as there
are 45 years left. This is highlighted by the overall results shown below:

Case 2 also has a larger impact compared to Case 1 due to the magnitude of

from this date which represents the lifetime of the solar panels.

resource consumption and time span and not the energy systems development.
4.2.8
Data Summary
The development in the LCA in the future is subject to decrease, which is
underlined by the sections above.
The results show that by calculating and
applying the DLCA method, the buildings with the ’longest’ lifetime also yields a
slightly lower CO2 emission, which conﬁrms that the more the pulses are ’spread’
out, over the years, the less impact it also has. Furthermore, the development
also shows that the dynamic impacts from the energy system aﬀect the emissions,
as the contribution from electricity and transportation transitions to net-zero
emissions impact, which is highlighted mostly in the period 2018-2030.
The
resource consumption factor is also applied, and it has a bigger eﬀect on Case
2 because it has a longer average building lifetime compared to the building
renovation strategies.

4.3

This section provides an overview of the renovation strategies’ eﬀects on the
energy frame for Case 1 and Case 2. Each case is analysed separately. The results
incorporate the energy factors 0.85 and 1.9 for heat and electricity, respectively,
to convert the energy required from building level to the energy system level.
The renovation strategies assume that the thermal properties for conventional
and reused materials are the same, and hence they are not distinguished in the
energy frame calculations.

Figure 4.22: Energy frame for each strategy in Case 1
Figure 4.22 illustrate the buildings energy frame when implemented each
renovation strategy.
The two horizontal lines represent the buildings current
energy frame where the red dotted line represents the heat consumption, and
the solid black lines represent the total energy frame, meaning the area between
these two lines is the electricity consumption.
80
==PAGE:97==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
The coloured shaded area illustrates the energy frame for the energy label ’A2010’
to ’D’. The ﬁgure establishes the building current energy performance as energy
label ’D’ and well above the limit to become ’C’.
Since Case 1 is a commercial building lighting is included in electricity
consumption, and it explains the relatively high share of electricity consumption.
In this case, there is a minimal share of overheating which is neglected in the
results and in the further work. It can be seen in Appendix N.3.
The strategies that combine energy-saving initiatives show the best outcomes,
with the energy frame being lowest for the strategy with ’150 mm insulation
of the exterior wall ﬁnished with part bricks and part timber’ combined with
’substituted of triple glazed windows’. This strategy combination results in a new
energy frame of 95.4 kWh/m2 which corresponds to energy label ’C’. However,
other exterior strategies with diﬀerent cladding materials, and same insulation
thickness and also with triple glazed windows showed only slightly deviating
performance.
In comparison, the same renovation strategy but without window substitution
consumes around 125 kWh/m2. This indicates that the windows are the most
signiﬁcant contributor to the energy frame in this particular case study. The
results indicate that the outer wall is already fairly insulated, making the extra
insulation redundant. The eﬀect on the energy frame from the diﬀerent facade
cladding types such as plaster, bricks, brick shells and timber, is insigniﬁcant
from the graphs. This suggests that the best-performing strategy needs to use
a holistic approach and thus look into other parameters such as costs, embodied
CO2 and lifespan.
The implementation of PV-panels has a minimal eﬀect on the electricity
consumption. This is due to the limited space on the roof and its ﬂat angle
of 13° making the PV-panels an ineﬃcient strategy.
A clear overview of the
energy-savings for each renovation strategy is presented in Appendix N.1.

Figure 4.23: Energy frame for each strategy in Case 2
The existing energy performance and all renovation strategies are illustrated,
similarly to Case 1. Additionally, an overview of energy-savings for each strategy

The current energy label for Case 2 is ’D’, and because it is a residential
building lighting is not included, and therefore the electricity consumption only
corresponds to building installations.
The ﬁgure conﬁrms this as electricity
consumption only accounts for a small share in the energy frame.
Opposed to Case 1, the implementation of PV-panels in Case 2 results in negative
electricity consumption, meaning higher production compared to consumption.
This is illustrated in Figure 4.23, where the energy frame improves with
more solar PV. Results indicate that heat consumption decreases with the
implementation of solar PV, however it remains constant and should be
interpreted as a decrease in the energy frame. In practice, the extra electricity
will be consumed by lighting and appliances, which was not accounted for in the
energy frame for Case 2.

==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 4. MODELLING RESULTS
By renovating with 150 mm exterior insulation and ﬁnish with plaster the energy
frame is reduced to around 46 kWh/m2, and an additional 11 kWh/m2 is reduced
by changing the windows to triple glazed.
The resulting energy label then
corresponds to ’B’. The strategy shows that insulation has the most signiﬁcant
impact on the energy frame, which suggests that the existing outer walls are
inadequately insulated. Furthermore, the windows were replaced in 2011, which
means that they are already performing satisfactorily.
When comparing the existing energy frame with the strategies with 80 mm,
120 mm and 150 mm exterior insulation and brick ﬁnishing. The energy-saving
for each strategy is 39.4 kWh/m2, 42.5 kWh/m2 and 43.9 kWh/m2 respectively,
meaning that the energy-saving stagnates with the increasing insulation. This
pattern is the same for all strategies regardless of the cladding material.
It is possible to combine the heat saving strategies and also implement solar PV,
which for the best performing strategies, will result in an energy label upgrade
from ’D’ to ’A2010’.
All data values for each renovation strategy on both building cases can be seen
in Appendix N.3 and N.4

With the results achieved for each topic, the next chapter applies the results to
assess how buildings can comply with the 1.5°C target.
Figure 4.24 illustrates how the results from the chapter are combined and the
interconnection between the results. The framework boundary uses the building
operation (from Section 4.3) to assess how much CO2 is released from operation
in 2018-2050 by considering dynamic electricity and district heating factors
(from Section 4.1.3.2) together with the renovation strategies that will reduce
the operational energy.
The renovation strategies requires an investment of
CO2 (from Section 4.2) which is assessed through consideration of electricity
and transportation factors for the speciﬁc year (from Section 4.1.3.1).
The
embodied neutralisation factor is multiplied on the embodied materials in the
framework. Furthermore, the framework is constrained by a boundary, deﬁned
by a limit value for diﬀerent building types derived from the roadmaps (from
Section 4.1.3.3).
The following chapter assesses the results of each renovation strategy by applying
the framework.

5.1
CO2 Framework
The results from the energy system, embodied carbon and operational energy
analysis, is incorporated into a framework to holistically assess the CO2 impact
of each strategy in relation to each other. The framework has a limit value that
represents, to what extent the CO2 emissions should be reduced.

illustrates how the roadmap results (from Section 4.1.3.3) are derived:

types are important to ﬁnd the decrease necessary for the speciﬁc budget.

with the following values for the period 2018-2050 to achieve the 1.5°C target:

The BAU scenario is the CO2 the building type emits between 2018-2050 if
no energy renovations and energy system improvements are made. The roadmap
scenario is the total targeted CO2 that the building emits through energy system
improvements and energy renovations.
Therefore the multistorey buildings with an energy label ’D’, has to decrease with
-60.8% in total CO2 emissions compared to its BAU scenario to meet the targets,
whereas commercial buildings with an energy label ’D’, needs to decrease with
-64.0%. This decrease represents the limit values and can be seen in Appendix
O.1-O.3.
The framework furthermore analyses price estimations for diﬀerent
strategies. The values applied, from the three topics, for the framework, can be
seen in Appendix P.

Figure 5.3: Total accumulated CO2 emissions from 2018 to 2050 depending on
renovation strategy and implementation year for Case 1. For enlargement of the

Figure 5.3 represents all strategies and the total accumulated CO2 emitted from
each building strategy implemented between 2018 to 2050. The x-axis is the
87
==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
implementation year of the strategy, and the y-axis is the total accumulated
tonnes CO2 emitted (in the period 2018-2050).
The black line illustrates the speciﬁc building’s total accumulated CO2 for the
whole period: when no renovation strategies are implemented, but only the
energy system is improving. It is ,therefore, an ’example’ of how much is emitted
without energy-saving strategies is a reference for comparison. The yellow line
represents the limit value of how much CO2 the speciﬁc building can emit to
comply with the 1.5 °C pathway. If no energy-saving initiatives are implemented
in the period, the building will emit 825.6 tonnes of CO2 which are 12% more
than the limit value of 736.1 tonnes, and means that actions are needed. In order
to comply with the 1.5°C temperature target, only strategies below the yellow
line are acceptable.
From the ﬁgure, it is clear that reducing the operational energy has a big inﬂuence
on the total CO2. The ﬁgure shows three patterns formed as ’belts’. The ﬁrst is
the least energy-saving strategy which is ’single strategies’ such as implementing
only PV, interior or exterior insulation. This strategy alone is not suﬃcient in any
implementation year, even if it reduces the building energy. The second belt from
the least energy-saving strategy is the same strategies but with a combination
of double-glazed windows. This strategy is suﬃcient with the limit value until
between 2023-2026. The third and most energy-saving strategy is the strategies
where triple-glazed windows are combined. This strategy generally performs the
best of all strategies.
From each ’belt’ there is a clear pattern between the ranking correlated to
the CO2 emissions of materials from the embodied carbon assessment.
The
operational energy is therefore more impactful in this building case compared to
embodied carbon. Generally, the results shows that the strategies with most
insulation also yields the overall lowest CO2, and furthermore the cladding
materials which are reused performs better than conventional materials. This
is evident for all ’belts’.
For speciﬁc examples, the implementation of solar PV strategies has a ’negative’
CO2 already after 2021 and 2022 for the strategies with 12 and 24 panels. This is
because the embodied carbon ’investment’ exceeds the total carbon saved from
the strategy in the time horizon, and the energy saved for PV-panels, therefore
becomes minimal simultaneously with the decreasing CO2-factor.
The CO2-factor for electricity is almost carbon-neutral in 2030, which gives the
PV strategies in the building a short period to save the embodied CO2 back.
88
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
Generally, for the embodied carbon, there is a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the
conventional and reused bricks where the embodied CO2 is around 30 tonnes
less in 2018 (see light green and dark green signatures in Figure 5.3).
The
diﬀerence, however, becomes smaller through time and in 2045 the diﬀerence
is only 5 tonnes.
This is partly due to the energy becoming cleaner, which
aﬀects the production and reprocessing of materials, but also because of the
relative embodied reduction, which is used in the framework. The embodied
neutralisation factor follows the industry’s process heating trend and assumes
equilibrium in CO2 release and absorption by 2050 where the CO2 is accounted
for as ’0’ (this will be further discussed in the next chapter). The values for total
embodied carbon and accumulated CO2 emission for each strategy in each year
can be seen in Appendix O.2 - O.5.
5.2.1
Application of Framework
The framework has been tested on Case 1 for six selected renovation strategies,
to examine their outcomes when implemented in 2018, 2025 and 2035.
The renovation strategies include an extra 150 mm exterior insulation ﬁnished
with either conventional or reused brick/timber cladding, with and without
combining a substitution to triple glazed windows. An additional 70 mm interior
insulation ﬁnished with reused gypsum will also be examined, to compare options
between exterior and interior renovations, also with and without windows. Lastly,
the strategy with 24 solar PV panels is tested.
The strategies’ impact on CO2 emissions, energy and costs are examined for the
implementation years 2018, 2025 and 2035.
Figure 5.4 shows the selected strategies and their total CO2 contribution for all
the strategies and the outcomes in the implementation years. The black line
represents how the building performs if no energy-saving initiatives are made
(described earlier) but the building only relies on the decreasing CO2-factors
for electricity and district heating. The dotted line represents the limit value
(described earlier), and these are the comparing measures for the renovation
strategies.

Figure 5.4: Case 1: CO2 emissions for selected strategies with implementation
year 2018, 2025 and 2035 in total accumulated tonnes CO2 emissions for the
building between 2018 and 2050
The operational emissions account for the highest share of emissions, and the
building will emit more CO2 by postponing the renovations. This is seen in the
ﬁgure, as an early implementation leads to lower CO2 emissions towards 2050,
although the embodied carbon will reduce by time.
When comparing the CO2 emissions amongst the renovations, it is evident that
implementation of additional 150 mm exterior insulation, ﬁnished with reused
bricks/timber, while also replacing the windows to triple glazed, is the best option
for Case 1. If implemented in 2018, the total CO2 emissions towards 2050 will
be 578 tonnes with 72 tonnes stemming from embodied carbon over the entire
period.
The corresponding strategy with conventional bricks assumes the same thermal
properties and thus have identical operational emissions, but it emits 27
tonnes more CO2 related to embodied emissions. The production of bricks is
connected to the most signiﬁcant embodied carbon emissions. Still, when used
with additional exterior insulation and replacements of windows, it lowers the
operational emissions enough to become the best strategy examined based on
climate impact in 2018.
90
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
Replacing the windows, but using an additional 70 mm interior insulation ﬁnished
with gypsum, leads to an almost equally good option. Where exterior bricks are
21.4% below the limit value, the interior solution is 20.5% below in 2018.The
ﬁrst strategy implements more exterior insulation which means it has a lower
U-value and also lowers the thermal bridges, and it leads to fewer operational
emissions. However, the production of more and heavy materials, compared to
gypsum, almost equalises the total emissions for the two strategies.Furthermore,
it is evident that, as the CO2-factors decreases for district heat and electricity,
the need for more insulation becomes more redundant which means that if the
strategies are implemented in 2025, the interior solution is preferable.
The corresponding strategies of both interior and exterior renovation, without
substitution of windows, show signiﬁcantly higher operational emissions. For
the exterior strategy, the diﬀerence is 224.5 tonnes CO2, while the reduction in
embodied carbon is only 41 tonnes in 2018. This conﬁrms that the windows have
a low energy performance, and suggests that the focus should be on replacement
of the windows. Figure 5.4 shows that none of the strategies is under the limit
value if the windows are not replaced, even for the implementation year 2018.
Earlier results indicate that the installation of solar PV only has a minimal eﬀect
on the operational energy for Case 1. The ﬁgure conﬁrms this, as the inclusion
of embodied emissions, keeps the total emissions around the same existing value
throughout the time horizon.
Generally, when evaluating the CO2 emissions for the six strategies, it is seen
that the strategies that replace windows stay beneath the limit value after 2025.
However, in 2035 it is too late for all strategies to comply with the limit value,
which means that action is needed early. Still, it is possible for the building to
reduce some of its CO2 emissions compared to the existing line.

Figure 5.5: Visualisation from the framework - Additional 150 mm exterior
brick/timber insulation + triple glazed windows implemented in 2025
Figure 5.5 shows an example of how the renovation strategies are visualised in
the framework.
This particular example shows how the buildings CO2 emission develops towards
2050 when implementing the previously mentioned exterior brick/timber incl.
windows strategy for the implementation year 2025.
The dotted black line illustrates how much CO2 the building can emit and still
comply with the budget derived from the goal of maximum 1.5°C temperature
rise. The orange line is the reference line and describes how the building CO2
emissions will develop when no energy-saving initiatives are implemented. The
green line represents the CO2 emissions pathway with the implementation of the
strategy. The peak illustrates the extra CO2 emitted from the embodied carbon
in the implementation year.
The drop after the renovation corresponds to around 7 kg/m2, making the
emissions stay well below the limit value. The steep decrease in emissions from
2027 to 2030 is due to the rapid reduction of CO2-factors for district heat and
electricity.

The strategies that replace the windows have the highest economic saving

saves almost 100,000 DKK/year, whereas the interior solution with gypsum

window replacements will not save an adequate amount of energy which can be
seen in the low savings and high return of investments. The investment cost for
solar PV is relatively low, so despite the minimal savings, this strategy has the
lowest return of investment of the examined strategies. The results add a new
perspective to the renovations, which will be discussed. The investment cost,

EXT PLA120 C
Figure 5.7: Total accumulated CO2 emissions from 2018 to 2050 depending on
renovation strategy and implementation year for Case 2. For enlargement of the
ﬁgure see Appendix O.7
The yellow line represents the limit value, which is found from the model for
multistorey buildings with energy label ’D’. For this particular building, the limit
value is 390.2 tonnes of CO2 from 2018 to 2050. If no energy-saving initiatives
are made (but only the energy system is improving) the building will emit 440.4
tonnes of CO2 in total between the period 2018 to 2050.
For all renovation strategies, there is a ’trend’ which slowly increases. This is due
to ’not’ making any implementations from 2018, but also due to the CO2-factors
for electricity, district heat and transportation having less impact transitioning
towards 2050.
From the renovation strategies’ LCA development, it was seen that most
materials never reaches ’0’ as they will always emit CO2 due to its ’base
94
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
contribution’.
Therefore the framework applies the ’embodied neutralisation
factor’ that accounts for the absorption of CO2 which will come from land
management practices, biochar sequestration and CO2 capture technologies (this
was not modelled in the energy system). The absorption will also become more
important and have a higher impact as the energy system transitions towards
renewable technologies. This will be discussed further in Section 6.5.3.
From Figure 5.7 two ’belts’ emerged.
The ﬁrst belt includes strategies with
conventional brick cladding and PV. These are the least energy-saving strategies
and does not comply with the limit value. This belt can be divided into two
’sub-belts’, the strategies with PV and double glazed windows together, and the
strategies with no window replacement and triple glazed windows. The embodied
carbon in the double glazed windows outweighs the saved operational carbon,
because of the relatively low U-value in the existing windows. In comparison,
the triple glazed windows are close to an equilibrium of embodied carbon versus
saved operational carbon. This concludes that window replacement is not as
beneﬁcial compared to Case 1, and the best strategy is determined, based on
insulation thickness and cladding material. This trend applies to all strategies
in both belts.
The strategies in the second belt are below the limit value.
The expiration
year for when the strategies comply with the limit varies from 2022 to 2028.
The strategy with 70 mm interior insulation appears to be the best, although
the operational carbon is higher compared to strategies with higher insulation
thickness and diﬀerent cladding. This is because of the low embodied carbon in
gypsum compared to the other cladding materials.
In general, all the exterior strategies emit less CO2 with thicker insulation in
2018. As time goes and the CO2-factor for district heat decreases, less insulation
is needed and the CO2, for embodied CO2, surpasses the saved operational CO2.
An example is the strategies with 80 and 120 mm insulation with conventional
brick cladding in 2027. The values for total embodied carbon and accumulated
CO2 emission for each strategy in each year can be seen in appendix O.8 - O.11.

Figure 5.8: Case 2: CO2 emissions for selected strategies with implementation
year 2018, 2025 and 2035 in total accumulated tonnes CO2 emissions for the
building between 2018 and 2050
Figure 5.8 shows the CO2 contribution of the six strategies in the implementation
years 2018, 2025 and 2035.
The embodied CO2 accounts for a larger share compared to Case 1, caused by
the neglection of electricity consumption for lighting, which is only included
in Case 1. The share of embodied CO2 decreases with time according to the
greener material production and the embodied neutralisation factor, and higher
operational CO2 emitted before implementing the strategy. Figure 5.8 shows
that the strategies with 70 mm interior insulation is the lowest CO2 emitter.
The diﬀerence in embodied carbon is almost the same, as the carbon saved
with the implementation of the triple glazed windows in 2018. With time the
embodied carbon will be more vital due to the rapid decrease in CO2-factors for
electricity and district heat, compared to a slighter decrease of emissions in the
production of materials. This can be seen in 2025 and 2035, where it is clear
that the strategy with 70 mm interior insulation has the lowest CO2 contribution.
The same pattern applies to the strategies with 150 mm exterior insulation with
96
==PAGE:113==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
reused brick cladding and with and without triple glazed windows. The strategies
with exterior insulation present the lowest operational carbon but eventually
gets outperformed by the interior insulation strategies due to the high embodied
carbon in bricks.
The high embodied carbon in conventional bricks is the reason that the strategy
is not under the limit in 2018. Whereas in 2035 it is the biggest CO2 contributor,
and even exceeds what the building emits without any strategies. The solar PV
system performs better for Case 2 compared to Case 1, which is explained by
a more suitable orientation, angle and more available roof area to install more
panels. The strategies with PV-system alone can not comply with the budget.
As in Case 1, the PV-system emits more CO2 compared to the building without
any implementation of strategies.

strategies for Case 2.

The replacement of window strategies has the highest yearly savings because

investment of 15 years. Again, solar PV has the lowest return of investment of 7
years, over twice as fast than in Case 1. The investment cost, savings and return
of investment for all strategies can be seen in Appendix O.12.
5.4
Summary
The need for energy-saving renovations in buildings is urgent, which applies to
both cases.
If waiting until 2027, the buildings are not able to keep within
the CO2 budget and are thus forced to become ’carbon negative’ in the future.
Alternatively, other sectors in the energy system are forced to reduce extra
emissions to compensate for the building sector.
There are some noticeable diﬀerences in the two cases.
The best strategies
for each case are each other’s opposite. Case 1, with insulated walls but old
windows, beneﬁts most from the strategies with window replacement, while Case
2, already has windows that are unfeasible to substitute. However, it has minimal
insulation, and therefore beneﬁts most from the strategies with high insulation
alone. In both cases, the PV strategies are the least favourable, considering CO2
emissions. However, PV has the fastest return of investment, which means it
can be combined with other heat-saving strategies to reduce the energy and thus
save money.
The diﬀerence between conventional and reused materials is relatively small for
timber and gypsum while it is more signiﬁcant for bricks. With the improvement
of the energy system, the incentive to energy-renovate buildings declines from a
CO2 emission point of view because the CO2 savings decrease over time.
The purpose of the framework is to synthesise the energy systems development,
embodied carbon and operational energy into one ﬁnal result that indicates
whether the current CO2 that is released from the building’s energy consumption,
reaches the net-zero emissions target by complying with the speciﬁc limit value.
If this is not the case, the framework suggests renovation strategies so buildings
can comply with the target.
98
==PAGE:115==
CHAPTER 5. FRAMEWORK RESULTS
Results show that choices of renovation strategies are not obvious, from the two
case studies. Case 1 suggests early implementation and focuses on reduction of
energy, as this had the biggest impact on the overall CO2. Case 2 has less focus
on reducing operational energy and more focus on the embodied material.

6.1
Main Findings
With an increasing rate of construction, renovation, and demolition coupled
with an enormous operational demand, the building sector is one of the most
prominent emitters of CO2 in the world. Current eﬀorts to curb CO2 emissions
are achieved either through looking into operational energy in buildings or by
focusing eﬀorts on minimising the materials’ embodied carbon.
Past studies
shows that improving buildings operational performance can yield optimum
sustainable results (Akande et al., 2016), and recovering materials from waste can
reduce the embodied energy in materials (Ng et al., 2015). Both of these studies
improve its short-term aspects when looking solely in an individual perspective,
however focusing solely on one perspective does not yield the most optimal
solutions, in a holistic context, which results from this study strongly suggests.
==PAGE:118==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
Past studies on energy systems modelling have shown that heat-saving initiatives
must be implemented as a part of its transitioning plan (Mathiesen et al., 2015).
This study conﬁrms that on a national scale, the building industry in Denmark is
recommended to follow a ’very ambitious’ energy-saving scenario as this results
in the lowest total cost for the energy system, to fulﬁl political climate targets
and to stay within the CO2 budget constraints. The roadmap results indicate
that the worst performing buildings such as ’G’, ’E’ and ’F’ has the highest
carbon budget and is therefore urgent to consider as it releases a large amount
of emissions in the Danish building sector. It furthermore has the highest heat-
saving potential per m2, and because they are the cheapest to renovate, the
heat-saving potential should be fulﬁlled rapidly for these buildings which are not
demolished.
For the study, two building with energy level ’D’ is assessed.
The results
from the study indicate that there is a clear, holistic correlation between the
national scale energy system, operational energy and embodied carbon when
assessing and comparing strategies that release the least amount of CO2 emissions
across the building sector in the studies’ time horizon. The results demonstrate
that it is possible to streamline renovation strategies for existing buildings to
adhere with the total energy systems net-zero emissions roadmap by setting a
limit value of CO2, obtained from the future energy system.
It furthermore
indicates that there are many options available for renovating existing buildings
with energy label ’D’. However, in the framework, whether the focus has to be
on reducing operational energy, or focusing on materials with a low embodied
carbon, is diﬀerent from each building case, which underlines that buildings must
be assessed individually. The results from the renovation strategies investigated,
suggests that renovations should be implemented early, as this will yield the
lowest amount of CO2 emissions.
The following sections will address the limitations and uncertainty connected to
each research topic and address the sensitivity of selected uncertainty parameters
for the results in the framework.
102
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.2
Energy System
The energy model was developed to investigate the outcomes of diﬀerent heat-
saving actions and on that basis, determine corresponding sectoral CO2-factors
and roadmaps for the building sector. As with outcomes from all models, the
results are a simpliﬁcation of reality. As described in ’McKinsey & Company’s’
article (Charumilind et al., 2020) models can be divided into three main
components: raw data, assumptions (that deﬁne what the model does with the
data), and ﬁnal output.
The uncertainties in the data have a big inﬂuence throughout the study and
model results, which challenges the model’s credibility. The data collection was
found from a limited number of sources to limit the uncertainties between data
sets. All models come with uncertainties, both foreseen and unforeseen. One
particular example can be pandemic’s such as the recent COVID-19 that have
sparked vital consequences and unforeseen circumstances on the economy, energy
investments and CO2 emissions. This for example highlights the simplicity of
models and how it represents reality which can quickly change. The uncertainties
and limitations are presented in the next section.
6.2.1
Limitations for the Energy Model of Denmark
In general a limitation of the energy systems model is that it only takes into
account CO2 emissions, based on IPCC’s report (Rogelj et al., 2018) and the
CO2 budget.
However, this is not to undermine other environmental impact
categories, such as other GWP gases, ozone depletion, acidiﬁcation and other
categories which are important to address. In a larger perspective, the model
will neglect, e.g. some environmental or societal aspects, as it purely assesses
energy-related CO2 emissions.
6.2.1.1
Land-Use
The agriculture sector and forest plantation for carbon uptake, are not a direct
part of the model, and more investigations could have been made for availability
of land-use. The available land is critical to assess because it involves area for
installation of wind and solar energy. Furthermore, the increasing demand for
103
==PAGE:120==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
biofuels also requires a portion of land. Additionally, there exists a so-called
’NIMBY-eﬀect’, which means less area for potential energy, which has caused
problems for local nuisances. This for example could not be addressed in the
model, but it is an important aspect for evaluation because it involves human
interference. There are numerous possible scenarios of how land will be used in
the future, even leading to moral questions if we should prioritise food or energy.
The model is constrained by ’Energy Agreement 2018’ of halving the number of
onshore wind turbines by 2030, so the land-use aspect has been considered in the
model. However, for solar panels, the land-use has been estimated to a certain
amount of area in the model, but this was assumed without dedicated a speciﬁc
location.
6.2.1.2
Sustainablity of Biomass
The energy system assumes that the consumption of biomass is carbon-neutral.
This harsh assumption requires that new plantation are at least equal to the
consumed biomass.
According to ’Klimarådet’ (Sørensen et al., 2018) it is
not always the case that biomass is replanted when combusted. Even if it is,
there will be many years between combusted emissions compared to the uptake
during replantation.
This can harm the climate, because it may ’overshoot’
the temperature limit, described in the Paris Agreement, during the carbon
uptake process.
Based on the national potentials of biomass, deﬁned in the
model, there is a need for importing biomass, and it is even harder to track
if the combusted biomass will be replanted from its original region.
Also, if
the emissions from transporting the biomass will be compensated with, e.g.
additional plantation. This leads to questions if it should be allowed to import
biomass to become carbon-neutral, and in general, reconsider the agreement for
sustainable biomass. The only alternatives to biomass as ’carbon-neutral’ options
are ﬂuctuating renewables, and as the model is dependant on biomass imports,
it would require a high increase in capacities of, e.g. wind and solar energy to
compensate for the stable availability.
6.2.1.3
New Technologies
The model only relies on mature technologies to become carbon neutral in 2050.
This is based on numerous assumptions about how eﬀective renewable energies
are, and that they can be implemented in, e.g. the industry and transport sector.
104
==PAGE:121==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
However, the research in new technologies develops and thus updates to the
model should be applied. For example, studies from ’Klimarådet’ (Klimarådet,
2020) explains that the use of Carbon Capture and Storage/Use (CCS and CCU)
could be a cost-eﬀective strategy towards the carbon-neutral target. CCS could
be implemented for biogas production and fossil-ﬁred power and heat production.
Additionally, it could help the challenging industrial processes to decrease its
emissions in alternative ways compared to the projection in this study.
The
critical transport sector could also have a diﬀerent outlook if CCU and the so-
called Power-to-X were to be implemented.
6.2.1.4
General Details Between Sectors
In an energy system, all sectors are directly or indirectly interrelated, which
means that the entire energy system was modelled to derive results for the
framework.
This study focuses on the building industry, meaning that the residential and
commercial sector was disaggregated into a higher degree of details compared
to the other sectors for a better basis for result management. The buildings
are characterised by a high share of district heating, which is uncertain. A more
comprehensive analysis of geographical areas with district heating potential could
provide a more realistic basis for heating types.
The scope of this study is not to evaluate the details in the entire energy system,
but rather to incorporate the energy balance to estimate the impact on the
building sector from the behavioural trends from other sectors. Further work
could include in-depth modelling of other sectors to reduce uncertainties in the
behaviour of the system. Some results from the transport sector and industry
sector are used as a basis for embodied carbon calculations, which includes
emissions from lorries and processes.
The transportation of materials is simpliﬁed to only cover lorries of one type. If
the model is expanded, it should cover more options for freighting materials and
also diﬀer between vehicle size, loads and long- and short distances. Also, the
industry sector can be disaggregated into smaller practices such as cement, steel
or wood manufacturing.
Further additions would provide a diﬀerent outlook
for the sectors, which also would include diﬀerent CO2-factors and embodied
neutralisation factor in our case. For example, the freight CO2-factor experiences
a rapid decrease towards 2030, and more study could provide a more accurate
basis for this.
105
==PAGE:122==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.2.2

This section performs sensitivity analysis for the energy system. As mentioned,
uncertainties are a substantial part of modelling. Sensitivity analysis will help
understand the eﬀect on the output when changing inputs. All the uncertainties
lead to an extreme number of combinations that could be examined. To limit
the scope of this part, only the consequences of changing the magnitudes of heat
savings will be analysed, as this is the focus of this study.

Figure 6.1: Sensitivity analysis for the heating system in 2050 based on the three

Figure 6.1 shows the sensitivity on the heating system for the moderate,

scenarios are 12.4, 17.6 and 20.9 TWh, respectively, in 2050. The ﬁgure includes

illustrates how the eﬀorts on heat-saving initiatives release some pressure on the
district heating production and also lowers the usage of individual boilers.
Commonly for all scenarios is, that the solar heating is using all of its capacity
deﬁned in the model. The production from individual heat pumps is also the

boiler production before it will implement more heat pumps.

Figure 6.2 shows the total average annual system costs for the three heat-saving
scenarios. It conﬁrms that the system should strive to curb the heat demand as
the ’very ambitious’ scenario has the lowest system cost. This means that the
savings on district and individual heating outweigh the investment costs for heat
savings deﬁned in the model. However, the savings in total system costs are not
as signiﬁcant as expected, with only 0.25% diﬀerence between the ’moderate’ and
’very ambitious’ scenario. One explanation could be that the extra investment
cost for expanding the district heating grid is not implemented in the model, and
that could make a more signiﬁcant impact between the scenarios.
Figure 6.3 shows how the total primary supply in 2050 decreases between the
scenarios as higher heat savings decrease the demand. It is noticed that the
supply from ﬂuctuating renewable energy are constant between the scenarios,
which means that the model adjusts the diﬀerent demands with biomass. The
’very ambitious’ scenario, with the lowest biomass supply, already utilises the
entire national biomass potential leading to imports.
This means that fewer
heat savings lead to more import of biomass, which conﬂicts the arguments
described in section 6.2.1.2, that high biomass consumption is a questionable
strategy forward.
The research based its heat-saving ambitions on past studies by Wittchen et al.
(2017), where the potential was based on buildings that needed renovations for
other reasons. However, it can be discussed if more heat-saving ambitions could
have yielded more beneﬁts to the overall system as points towards clear beneﬁts
in the heat saving sensitivity analysis. This is however, a diﬃcult assess whether
is realistic practically but can be set up for further research. Furthermore, in an
economic perspective, there is a tipping point at to which renovation investments
exceeds the economic savings leading to questions regard economic feasibility
Mathiesen et al. (2015).

6.2.3
Model Validation
There are numerous variations and scenarios for the outcomes of energy models,
as they depend on assumptions and what they are showcasing. While models
can challenge each other’s outlooks for a future system, they can also validate
amongst each other to limit uncertainties and pitfalls. This section compares
a selection of parameters from critical technologies for power and heating
generation. This is a relevant section as the energy model has a high volume
of simpliﬁed sectors, and are thus subject to a high share of limitations which
questions its credibility. The DEA has made diﬀerent energy scenarios (Danish
Energy Agency, 2014) where this part compares the TIMES model with one
scenario that forecasts a high share of wind energy and another scenario with a
high share of biomass consumption.
