6.3
Embodied Carbon
One of the challenges of assessing embodied CO2 is the lack of information on
technological development and how it aﬀects future processes (Gantner et al.,
109
==PAGE:126==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
2018).
The research on embodied carbon in materials addresses this lack of
information by incorporating the energy systems development each year and how
it decreases with time. The overall approach is to manually deduct the amount of
electricity and transportation processes for each strategy from its LCI, and then
multiply it with the new CO2-factors. This approach is, however a simpliﬁcation
of how the materials will develop in the future. The study does not consider
the perspective of how the manufacturing and production processes of materials
will become more eﬃcient in the time frame. It only considers how the building
components and materials become more eﬃcient related to the fuel mix from the
power and transportation sector.
According to the framework, the results indicate that there is a need to innovate
and create materials with a lower embodied CO2 combined with better energy
characteristics than in the renovation strategies researched. This makes sense as
the strategies researched are entirely based on materials from 2018, and these
materials are therefore not suﬃcient up until 2050. This is especially important
as the energy systems model requires energy renovations up until 2050. The
current range of renovation strategies are according to the framework only able
to comply with the limit value until 2026 (Case 1) and 2028 (Case 2). This
issue calls for continued innovation in circular economic solutions with more
product oﬀerings and application of its principles across the production chain.
The research for example, relies on conventional solutions for windows as well
as on insulation materials, and this could be an important factor to further
research in order to get entirely reused strategies.
If targets are to be met,
and renovation strategies can continue beyond the dates, the focus has to shift
towards innovation on these components as well as creating materials with much-
improved energy qualities from today’s standards. The framework can in many
ways work towards answering these questions, such as how improved building
materials will need to become in the future, to live up to requirements of the
1.5°C target and this opens up an entirely diﬀerent ﬁeld which can be researched.
6.3.1
Lifetime
In speciﬁc terms, the lifespan of materials and components are subject to a lot of
uncertainty as there is a range of parameters which aﬀects the lifetime, such as
material quality, climate impact, usage, maintenance, aesthetics etc. In relation
to this, there are diﬀerent types of lifespans to consider, which vary in terms of
purpose and subjectivity (Aagaard et al., 2013).
110
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
The study applies a building’s average lifespan in Denmark in context with the
case studies. Residential buildings were found to have an average lifetime of 120
years, and commercial buildings with an average lifetime of 100 years, according
to Aagaard et al. (2013). In the study, the lifetime ’left’ for each building is
limited to the knowledge of the building’s construction year only.
In reality,
buildings can live longer if it has not been aﬀected by the mentioned lifetime
parameters. The strategies also have the potential to ’extend’ the lifetimes of
the buildings, which is not considered in the current study, due to lack of expert
knowledge, inspection and empirical data.
For building parts, each strategy is simpliﬁed to component level instead of
looking into each layer and its unique lifetime. The study was constrained to
component level due to lack of knowledge and data on building practices for each
layer. The interior gypsum strategy, for example, consists of a paint layer, which
has a diﬀerent lifetime than the rest of the components. This means that if the
strategies are looked into on a layer level, the resource consumption and LCA
would subsequently change and become more accurate.
To address the aﬀect of renovation strategies in terms of embodied CO2, the
research applies a dynamic LCA approach developed by Levasseur et al. (2010).
This approach takes into account lifetime’s of the buildings. A simpliﬁcation is
made on pulse emissions with all emissions subject to pulses in the same years but
is diﬀerentiated for both buildings of 45 and 58 years. These results in relation
to the DLCA, however, does not yield a big diﬀerence between both case studies.
The most decisive factor in regards to lifetime is the building components lifetime,
in relation to the building time left, as it outlines the resource consumption. The
uncertainty is connected to windows that are assumed to have a lifetime of 50
years, and solar PV panels on the roof with a lifetime of 30 years. The windows
and solar PV panels rely on the single-point estimate, however the lifetime again
can vary from supplier to supplier. The variations of a lifetime for both building
components will be tested in the sensitivity analysis
6.3.2
Circular Economy
Circular economic initiatives, such as reused materials, are superior in the
research case compared to its conventional equivalent. The study assumes that
both strategies have the same lifetime as well as thermal qualities. Whether this
is possible in the future is diﬃcult to assess. In theory, it will always be possible
111
==PAGE:128==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
to create products with the same characteristics, but whether the products are
feasible to reproduce with exact qualities, as its equivalent, is another matter.
Furthermore, the study does not look into other circular approaches in its
production chain, such as recycling.
This is because of the lack of products
available on the market as well as data from studies and production practices
for recycled materials as highlighted by Tingley et al. (2018) and Andersen
et al. (2019). The production of materials is a ﬁxed derivation of the energy
consumption related to production practices of today, as well as the materials
energy qualities are derived from products on today’s market.
The study
is therefore constrained by simple conventional materials and simple reused
materials that are assumed to not require a lot of reprocessing.
In a diﬀerent perspective, there has been preliminary research on principles such
as ’Urban Mining’ in the building industry (Koutamanis et al., 2018), which
investigates how to use resources in an urban context. According to the results
from the framework, this approach can create much value to reach the targets,
as it will bring more options for renovation strategies with a very low embodied
CO2 due to materials being ’mined’ in the cities.
As mentioned before, the
framework demands superior materials in the future, or else it cannot reach
its climate targets. This means that more focus should shift towards circular
economic principles for materials, which must be incorporated almost entirely
into the building sector very soon.
However, there were some principles which, did not ﬁt well with the framework.
It was noted from the analysis that principles such as ’Design for Deconstruction’
had very little eﬀect on the materials, as the beneﬁts would only happen after
the buildings end of life.
Past studies furthermore suggest that this paradigm shift is diﬃcult because
resource recovery and resource eﬃciency for reused building materials lack
accuracy and practicability (Hossain et al., 2018). Reusing materials has clear
beneﬁts but whether it can be applied more into the industry is diﬃcult to assess
and requires further research.
6.3.3
General limitations and future work
Windows were generalised in the LCA and were measured in m2 with a ﬁxed
frame factor. However, smaller windows have a bigger frame factor compared to
112
==PAGE:129==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
bigger windows if the frame width is ﬁxed (Carmody et al., 2012). Therefore,
some limitations on the window type must be looked into from each case to know
the exact type of window and its consequent frame factor.
Furthermore, there were also limitations regarding the location of production and
the production itself for each material. All materials are based on production in
Denmark highlighted by the electricity and transportation factor from Denmark.
For reused materials, this makes sense as it can be assumed that reused materials
are sourced domestically. Material production is, however more complex, and
cannot be limited to Denmark alone as raw materials or components can be
gathered from diﬀerent sources around the world. This was clearly noticeable
when constructing the LCI for each renovation strategy (ecoinvent).
For future studies, additional renovation strategies such as roof insulation and
ground slab insulation could be looked into, because these were ignored in the
research as they did not ﬁt both case studies. Both renovation strategies are
however, common in the building sector (Center, 2011).
Furthermore, the eﬀect of demolition or removal could also be looked into in
renovation, if materials were substituted or removed, which was not the case for
most of the renovation strategies that were primarily building extensions. There
could, for example, be cases where a combination of demolishing and renovation
was needed rather than a building extension.
6.4
Operational Energy
The operational energy is currently the biggest CO2 contributor through the
buildings entire lifespan.
Therefore a reduction of this is crucial to reduce
the emission and relieve the pressure on the energy systems to reach net-zero
emissions.
When discussing the building´s energy consumption, it is essential to diﬀerentiate
between the buildings’ technical energy consumption and its actual consumption.
The buildings’ technical energy consumption is the energy used for the building
to operate while the actual consumption, is adjusted to user-behaviour and
needs, which includes lighting and appliances. Measurements can only establish
the actual consumption while the buildings’ technical energy consumption is
calculated, focusing on the energy frame. In a commercial building, the lighting
113
==PAGE:130==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
is included in the buildings energy consumption, because it is a necessity, for
the use of the building. The energy frame is based on standards and does not
consider individual needs.
This makes buildings comparable with the use of
energy labelling.
In a renovation proposal, the energy saved is equal to money saved, and it is a
factor when deciding a proposal. Since the energy saved is calculated concerning
the energy frame, it can vary from the actual consumption when in use, and that
can be misleading for the customer. A past study describes some parameters
eﬀects on energy consumption when adjusted to individual needs. Furthermore,
it suggests that buildings actual heat consumption can vary up to 33% with a
temperature change of three °C (Kragh et al., 2017).
The electricity produced by PV-panels depends on the direct and indirect solar
radiation. Therefore the orientation and angle, the panels are installed in, is
critical. From the two cases, it is clear that the angle and orientation aﬀect
the production of electricity. When installing PV-panels the actual consumption
varies each year due to the weather, furthermore shading from new buildings or
trees aﬀect the eﬃciency of the PV-panels.
6.4.1
Limitations
For both cases, the accuracy of the energy consumption is uncertain, due to the
assumptions where gathered data was not suﬃcient. The assumptions are based
ON the building practice on the time of construction and with our best expert
knowledge.
The data collected for Case 1 is original and digital drawings and a 3D-Revit
model along with original building descriptions. The original and digital ﬂoor
plans show that the use of the building has changed. This suggests that data
found from the original practice of building is not necessarily still in practice
today, causing uncertainties for data only obtainable from the original drawings
and descriptions (for example the heating system.)
For Case 2, the gathered drawings are hand-drawn and scanned digitally, which
causes the drawings to be unclear.
Basic building data such as areas, wall
thickness, and building height are not directly obtainable from the drawings
and is measured with software calibration tools, making the values uncertain.
114
==PAGE:131==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
Furthermore, there are discrepancies between the drawings compared to how
the building is today.
For example, there are no windows on the gable in
the building, but they are found on the drawings.
With these apparent
diﬀerences, the credibility of the drawings can be questioned. Calculations on
The energy consumption is based on the drawings but corrected where there are
disagreements.
More accurate energy consumption could have been made with further
examination of the cases such as on-site building inspection as well as up-to-
date drawings in relation to how the building is today.
The calculated consumption for both cases is compared, with the actual
consumption data, to validate the results.
As discussed earlier, the actual
and calculated consumption will always vary, and this will be addressed in
the sensitivity analysis (Unfortunately, this report is not allowed to publish
the energy consumption data since it requires the accept from the building
administration).
6.5
Framework
The CO2 framework is a strategy tool that is essentially streamlining input and
carbon balancing. The limitations and uncertainties in the framework are mostly
connected to each topic, which is mentioned in earlier sections of the discussion
chapter. The framework for example applied a limit value, which was based on
the CO2 emissions roadmap and the buildings current energy consumption. The
uncertainties that relate to this is, therefore, the issue regarding actual and real
energy consumption and the CO2 allocation method (will be addressed in section
6.5.3).
The framework is a tool that purely assesses CO2 emissions, and that can lead
to some decisions lacking perspective.
For example, solar PV strategies are
not favourable in the framework. This is because in terms of CO2 saved from
electricity production, and CO2 invested in the material production of PVs, does
not have a big diﬀerence in the total carbon balancing. But it has a crucial
inﬂuence on the energy system which can not be portrayed in the framework.
Solar PVs are important for the system because according to the energy systems
model, it will gain a higher share in the system therefore implementing solar PV
115
==PAGE:132==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
on buildings are crucial. The same applies for the heat-saving strategies, where
the framework shows that the renovations do not beneﬁt the speciﬁc building
after a certain year. However, the energy model shows that the system should
heat save throughout the entire period. This shows the contradictions between
quantitative and qualitative analysis, where more work could address some of
these problems.
The framework is a decision making tool limited to the individual building case.
But its decision making has certain limitations. By implementing an economic
perspective on the framework results, solar PV panels, for example, yielded the
best results in regards to return on investment for both cases. This perspective
makes solar PV more favourable even if it does not reduce the CO2 as much
compared to other strategies, however for the energy system, it is crucial, so
combining solar PV with other strategies can subjectively also be an optimal
solution.
For the study, the limit value is derived based on the average heat-savings for
entire building stock (for each type). This means that for the building cases, the
limit value represents the average heat savings that each building type should
comply with. However, a more realistic assumption could be made on possible
renovation stock leading to tighter limit values due to the buildings having to
compensate for the stock there is not renovated.
6.5.1
Sensitivity Analysis on Parameters
For operational energy, there are uncertainties related to calculated and actual
heating consumption and furthermore uncertainties in electricity for solar PV
with the orientation, shadows and weather conditions having an inﬂuence on the
results. For embodied carbon, there are uncertainties related to the lifetimes of
materials and the subsequent resource consumption.
The following tornado plot addresses the sensitivity of these parameters and how
they aﬀect the framework:

Figure 6.4: Sensitivity analysis on Case 2 strategies with triple glazed windows
(left) and solar PV (right). Tornado plot on selected parameters showing the

Figure 6.4 shows the sensitivity on a strategy applying ’windows’ and a strategy
applying ’solar PV’. The lifetimes were tested for both strategies, while heat
savings were tested for the window strategy and electricity saving tested for
solar PV. The parameter values are incorporated as ’best guesses’ as seen above.
The lifetime parameter has a lot of uncertainty for the windows as seen on the
left ﬁgure. Windows are assumed to be available for an average lifetime of 50
years, according to Aagaard et al. (2013). If windows are only available for 40
years, the framework showed a deviation of approximately 4%.
The heating
is another uncertain parameter as it varies with user behaviour. The heating
parameter yielded a deviation of approximately 5% in the framework if the
heating consumption was 10% higher. This must be further addressed as the
empirical research by Kragh et al. (2017) outlines, approximately 3 out of 4
buildings deviates with 10-33% of the projected heating consumption.
For solar PV the sensitivity of lifetimes yielded the largest eﬀects but only
with 4% from the 10 year reduction of solar panel lifetime.
Deviation from
electricity consumption yielded even lower deviations due to the relatively low
energy consumption compared to heating.

Figure 6.5: Types of allocation methods and variations of heat saving scenarios.
The ﬁgure displays the number of renovation strategies (black value) complying
with the (CO2-factor and method-dependent) limit value and the year the last
renovation strategy complies with the limit value (white value)
The framework in the thesis is based on the ’very ambitious’ heat saving scenario
applying the 125% method for allocation of emissions from CHP plants for heat
and electricity. A methodology report by Energinet.dk (2019) underlines four
diﬀerent methods that can be applied to assess the CO2 from CHP plants.
Sensitivity analysis was tested on the framework results by applying these
methods.
The results applied the exact same strategies for both case studies.
Case 1
had 1650 renovation strategies based on the 33 years, whereas Case 2 had
1683 renovation strategies. The energy-content method in Case 1, for the ’very
ambitious’ scenario, allows for 552 strategies with the last strategy complying
with the limit value in 2035.
There is a notable diﬀerence compared to the
200% method, which allows 164 strategies with the last implementation year in
2025. For Case 2, for example, the same heat-saving scenario only allows for 62
strategies with the last implementation year in 2023.
Regardless of methods and scenarios, there is an urgent need to decarbonise
buildings which are indicated by the sensitivity analysis. The test shows large
variations between the results as the methods changes the CO2-factor and the
limit value. The main reason for the deviations of strategies complying with
the limit value is because of the limit value being CO2-factor dependent and the
118
==PAGE:135==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
CO2-factor being allocation-method dependent. On a national level, the CO2
budget (representing the national limit value) remains the same. However, for
each allocation-method and scenario, the budget shifts on a sectoral level leading
to diﬀerent limit values for the speciﬁc building (see Figure 5.2 from previous
chapter).
This highlights the importance of the choice of allocation-method
on a national level as it has a big inﬂuence on the framework. The choice of
125% method for allocation seems to be applied in most of the energy systems
projections going forward and was thus assumed to be the most presentable
(Energistyrelsen, 2012; Energistyrelsen, 2014). The problem is, however, only
applicable for buildings with district heating which was the case for both
buildings that is studied. Buildings with individual boilers, are not subject to
the deviation as the CO2-factor of gas (which is its fuel for heating) is constant.
6.5.3
Embodied Neutralisation Factor
One major assumption in the thesis is the deﬁnition of reaching net-zero
emissions. The net-zero emissions target, deﬁned by IPCC (Rogelj et al., 2018)
relates to the level where all CO2 emissions that are released is also absorbed. The
thesis only investigates what is released and assumes that the net-zero emissions
target is reached in 2050 and for the ’Power & Heat’, ’Residential’ (operation)
and ’Commercial’ (operation) sector by complying with the 1.5°C target set by
the model. This is possible in operation because the energy produced is made
of renewable energy that contains zero CO2 in its generation. But all sectors
require some sort of construction containing embodied carbon, for example for
the construction of wind turbines, buildings and vehicles. The sectors containing
embodied carbon can, therefore arguable never become net-zero emitters and
thus the rationale to reach net-zero emissions in a holistic system is by relying
on the absorption of CO2. The absorption is assumed to be through actions
such as land management practices, biochar sequestration, and CO2 capture
technology (Creamer et al., 2015). As an extension of this, ﬁgure 6.6 illustrates
the assumption in the study:
119
==PAGE:136==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
5
5
2018
2050
(+) Emissions release from operations (All sectors)
(+) Emissions release from embodied (All sectors)
(-) Emissions absorption from carbon capture* 
Net-zero emissions
Carbon neutralisation factor
Figure 6.6: Example of emissions from sectors’ operation and embodied emissions
release.
Embodied carbon release is absorbed through land management
practices,
biochar sequestration,
and CO2 capture technology.
Carbon
neutralisation factor is multiplied on embodied emissions
The study relies on an embodied neutralisation factor to abate the embodied
materials from buildings. It is multiplied in the framework to reach net-zero
emissions as deﬁned by the model.
This factor is applied to simplify the
equilibrium between emitted and absorbed CO2. The embodied neutralisation
factor follows the decarbonisation from industrial process heat deﬁned in Section
4.1.3.1.
There is a lot of uncertainty related to this factor since the factor furthermore
relies on allocation methods for absorption for each nation. Projecting how much
can be absorbed will depend on how much is released globally, and since it can
both come through natural and technological processes (Creamer et al., 2015),
it can be hard to predict and will require further research.
6.5.4
Summary and Further Work
The topic of decarbonising buildings in Denmark is not straightforward and
cannot be isolated to building level alone, but needs to be examined as a part
of a holistic system. As a consequence, the thesis decomposes and uniﬁes three
120
==PAGE:137==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
topics and develops a decarbonisation framework that takes into account how
an existing arbitrary building in Denmark can work towards achieving common
political goals. The results show that buildings can beneﬁt from a lower CO2
emission throughout the assessment period of 2018-2050, if it acts early and does
the necessary renovations strategies, that are informed by the framework results.
There are, however, uncertainties which inﬂuence the framework and its results.
The uncertainty in parameters such as a material’s lifetime and the CO2-factors
for electricity and district heating is not critical.
However, for the choice of
CO2 allocation method, the results indicate that there are signiﬁcant deviations
between each method.
Therefore in the future, a common standard method
will be beneﬁcial as it will ease and disclose comparisons. The 125% method is
however recommended and applied as it was the most presentable for the CHP
plants in Denmark (Energistyrelsen, 2014).
Generally each topic can improve and be reﬁned with access to more data. The
energy system is a simpliﬁcation and can improve by testing more scenarios to
develop more perspectives. The embodied carbon can extend its list of renovation
strategies and generally improve accuracy with access to data which is limited to
reused materials. Operational energy can improve in terms of testing mechanical
installations and the indoor environmental aspect, which is not considered much
in the study as the renovation strategies were done at a design level phase.
Furthermore, certiﬁcation schemes in the building industry often incorporate
essential parameters such as environmental, social and economic aspects. This
is, for example, a pivotal part of DK-GBC’s mission and is essential to consider
and something this research topic could go into depth with.
The initial goal, in the beginning, was to also assess new buildings besides looking
into existing buildings CO2, and furthermore to compute renovation strategies
and newly builts by making empirical research on a larger volume of buildings
by applying computation with CO2 as a design parameter. This was, however
unrealistic with the timeplan as it required a whole diﬀerent framework and
to build up empirical data and methods for assessing CO2.
We believe that
working with a framework for exactly newly built could lead to results with a
more balanced approach of operational energy as well as embodied material,
completely diﬀerent from the research. This is because the operational energy
could always be decided from the ﬁrst sketch, and the materials would therefore
have a very important aspect for consideration.
121
==PAGE:138==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
For further ideas moving forward, the thesis scope and goal gives possibilities
and rise to additional topics that can be researched. Some of the ideas that were
thought of could be looked upon externally during the research process was the
following:
• Developing a CO2 framework that could facilitate mitigation options and
possibilities for newly built
• Applying the CO2 as a holistic design parameter in the early design phase
for newly built through computational design
• Applying the framework on a large volume of existing buildings to assess
overall strategies (and on past renovations)
• Applying the framework as a tool to assess how building materials
embodied CO2 and its energy speciﬁcations should develop in the future
• Applying the framework to develop future building standards and
regulations on CO2
These topics can use this research paper, its methods and framework as
a reference or inspiration,
to further investigate a holistic approach for
decarbonising buildings in Denmark.
122
==PAGE:139==
Chapter
￿
Conclusion
This study aimed to investigate how the building industry can reach the net-
zero emissions target, speciﬁed in the ’Energy Agreement 2018’, within a carbon
budget deﬁned by IPCC. The research demonstrated that it is necessary to use
a holistic approach, as the evaluation of individual carbon abatement initiatives
may overlook more favourable solutions for the environment.
Through the art of cross-disciplinary practice, the study synthesised three
substantial
parts,
which
included
energy
modelling,
LCA
analysis
and
architectural planning, to produce a CO2 framework. This framework tool can
help in understanding the interaction between buildings’ operational carbon,
related to energy-use and embodied carbon, associated with the production and
implementation of building materials in the examined renovations strategies.
The results indicated that actions for energy savings,
through building
renovations and using appropriate combinations of strategies are urgent to
satisfy the environment.
The symbiosis with the energy system provided
beneﬁcial foresight on how the building industry should behave to meet an ideal
combination of renewable transition and relieving pressure on the national energy
system through energy-savings strategies. When considering the development in
the energy system, and embodied emissions, it was clear that more insulation
was not necessarily equal to optimal environmental solutions, despite additional
energy reductions. The CO2 emissions related to production, implementation
and end-of-life should be a part of the considerations when performing energy
renovations as these quantify to a signiﬁcant volume.
Furthermore, as the
==PAGE:140==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION
transition towards renewable energy production happens fast, the embodied
emissions have an increasingly substantial role.
The urge to explore how buildings behaved in an interrelated energy system
was the reason for developing the energy model.
Through energy modelling,
it was possible to decipher IPCC’s global CO2 budget and narrow it down to
practise it in the building sector to provide roadmaps for speciﬁc building types.
Moreover, the energy model made it possible to explore tailored scenarios, which
included speciﬁc heat-saving potentials for buildings and how it aﬀected the other
energy sectors. Outputs from additional supply and demand sectors were thus
examined to provide relevant dynamic CO2-factors which included, e.g. factors
for electricity and district heat. These were vital inputs for the framework to
evaluate the eﬀect of the future system.
The energy model also provided useful information to evaluate embodied CO2
emissions as production and transportation of materials are related to the
developments in the power, industry and transport sector. The implementation
of embodied emissions in the framework was crucial as this have an increasingly
signiﬁcant environmental impact, and it inﬂuenced the optimal choice of a
renovation strategy to a high degree. The LCA analysis of various materials
indicated an urgency of transitioning from the traditional linear economy
approach to a circular economy. It was clear from the framework results that the
renovation strategies did not live up to the future renovations after 2028. This
was partly due to the strategies energy qualities, but also due to the materials
having a high environmental impact. All results for embodied carbon, therefore,
points towards the same conclusions. Reusable materials will become a pivotal
element when renovating and designing the future of buildings moving towards
2050.
The results for operational energy was pivotal in terms of CO2 reduction for the
building cases. On a building level, the results clearly show that the operational
energy has high importance early in the assessed time period. This was due
to greener national energy system. Thus if no strategies were made early, the
building was subject to more CO2 emissions. On a building level, the results
showed that there was not necessarily a correlation between the operational
energy reduction magnitude and the strategy. The case studies concluded that
diﬀerent strategies also yielded diﬀerent solutions, and therefore it was necessary
to apply the framework in order to consider the aspect of embodied emissions.
Furthermore, on a national level, it is important to reduce the operational
demand in building as it will support the transitions towards net-zero emissions.
124
==PAGE:141==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION
The construction of the framework was an exploratory process and was related
to a vast amount of data and assumptions, which, as a consequence, was subject
to a high share of uncertainties and limitations. Based on these conclusions,
practitioners should consider implementing more accuracy to the energy model,
the LCA analysis and the architectural planning to improve inputs for the
framework.
However, its tendencies clearly state the importance of holistic
evaluations and articulate the need to expand the focus from only focusing
on energy savings also to consider the environmental impact when performing
renovations.
125
==PAGE:142==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION

Appendix
B
CO￿-factorFromCogenerationPlants
Energy Content Method
Share for Heat =
Heat Production
Heat Production + Electricity Production ú Total Fuel Consumption
Share for Electricity =
Electricity Production
Heat Production + Electricity Production ú Total Fuel Consumption
Thermal Eﬃciency Method
Share for Heat =
Heat Production
Thermal Efficiency
Share for Electricity = Total Fuel Consumption ≠Share for Heat
Energy Quality Method
Share for Heat =
Heat Production ú 0.15
Electricity Production + Heat Production ú 0.15 ú Total Fuel Consumption
Share for Electricity =
Electricity Production
Electricity Production + Heat Production ú 0.15 ú Total Fuel Consumption
Empirically, it is assumed that 1 kWh heat substitutes 0.15 kWh electricity.

Appendix
C
CarbonBudget
weighing principle
Formula
Population
Carbon budget = Country population (2017)
World population (2017)
GDP
Carbon budget = Country GDP (2017)
WorldGDP (2017)
Emissions
Carbon budget = Country CO2 emissions (2017)
World CO2 emissions (2017)
Historical emissions
Carbon budget = Country historical CO2 emissions (1960≠2017)
World historical CO2 emissions (1960≠2017)

Figure J.6: CO2 roadmap for detached residential and buildings divided in energy
labels in kg CO2 per m2
The building types ’E’ and ’F’ for detached buildings do not act as expected with
type ’E’ being more CO2-intensive compared to ’F’ in 2022. This is explained
by the fuel mix in the model, where ’F’ buildings are phasing out boilers with
fossil fuels faster. These buildings are primarily being replaced with district heat,
which means an expansion of the grid is needed.

ledende. Oplysninger, der fremgår
af nærværende tegning, er ikke
nødvendigvis fuldstændig i over-
ensstemmelse med de faktiske
forhold. Det er rådgivers forplig-
telse (evt. entreprenør i forbindel-
se med eksempelvis funktionsud-
bud) at kvalitetssikre tegningens
oplysninger forud for projektering
og udførelse. Rådgiver (eller evt.
entreprenør) må ikke udelukkende
basere sig på nærværende teg-
ning, men skal verificere de for
opgaveløsningen relevante
